Of course it also assists in the notification of infectious diseases and pulmonary tuberculosis. Metropolitan Borough of Poplar. Comprising the Registration Sub-districts of Poplar, Bromley and Bow. ANNUAL REPORT OF THE MEDICAL OFFICER OF HEALTH. To the Mayor, Aldermen and Councillors of the Metropolitan Borough of Poplar. Sir and Gentlemen, In accordance with the "Sanitary Officers' (London) Order, 1891," of the Local Government Board, dated 8th December, 1891, I have the pleasure of submitting to you my seventeenth Annual Report, which Report deals with the year 1909, and includes the Annual Reports of the Sanitary Inspectors, also the Report of the Lady Inspector and that of the Sanitary Inspector engaged in enforcing the bye-laws for Houses let in Lodgings or occupied by members of more than one family. The Report of the Lady Health Visitor is also included. VITAL STATISTICS. Population. The population of the Borough, estimated to the middle of the year 1909, on the theory of the basis of the rate of change between the censuses of 1896 and 1901 being maintained, is 170,688:— Registration London. Poplar. Bromley. Bow. 4,833,938 60,804 66,685 43,199 being 60.6 inhabitants per acre in Registration London, 73.32 per acre for the Borough of Poplar, 52.13 per acre in Poplar Sub-District, 110.00 B 2 in Bromley, and 77.73 in Bow.* These estimated populations are the figures used in calculating the various rates in the present Report for the year 1909. The various rates quoted in this Report for the years 1893 to 1900 are the rates as stated in previous Reports; they are calculated upon the then estimated populations, and as far as possible from past Annual Reports for the Sub-District of Bow figures respecting certain diseases have been extracted and the rates worked out so that the three sub-districts might be compared, but the rates in Tables IV., V., and VI., have been calculated upon revised estimated populations based upon the known censuses of 1891, 1896, and 1901. Births. During the year 1909, 134 births were notified outside the Borough belonging thereto:—132 by the Medical Officer of Stepney, one by the Medical Officer of Health of Paddington, and one by the Medical Officer of Health of Lambeth. 120 of the Stepney births occurred in the Mothers' Home, Commercial Road, and 12 in the London Hospital. The birth in Paddington took place in the London Lock Hospital. The Lambeth birth occurred in the Clapham Maternity Hospital. Mothers' Home, Commercial Road. poplar. bromley. bow. Male. Female. Male. Marriages. During the year the marriages of 2750 persons were registered in the Borough, giving an annual rate of 16.11 per 1000 of the estimated population, the rate for England and Wales being 14.6* and or London 15.8.† The annual marriage rate per 1000 living being:— Poplar and Bromley. County of London. 1899 17.90 — — — 1900 18.36 — — — 1901 18.7 23.6 19.9 17.6 1902 14.3 21.0 15.9 17.8 1903 16.6 19.9 17.4 17.4 1904 16.9 19.6 17.6 17.0 1905 16.2 18.3 16.7 16.9 1906 16.8 17.9 17.1 17.1 1907 17.01 18.93 17.49 17.0 1908 14.90 15.93 15.19 15.9 1909 16.23 15.74 16.11 15.8 Deaths. In the 52 weeks 2779 deaths were registered in the Borough of Poplar, 695 deaths having occurred in the Sub-District of Poplar, 1685 in Bromley, and 399 in Bow; from the 695 deaths which took place in Poplar there must be deducted 70 deaths of non-residents, and to the remainder must be added 383 deaths which occurred in outlying institutions, making a total of 1008 deaths of Poplar parishioners. Treating the 1685 registered deaths in Bromley in the same manner, viz., deducting 778 deaths of nonparishioners and adding 213 deaths in outlying institutions, the deaths to be assigned to this sub-district will be found to be 1120. From the 399 Bow deaths, there must be deducted 4 non-residents and added 279 deaths in outlying institutions, making a total of 674 deaths. In dealing separately with the parishes as above, it will be seen by referring to Tables I., II. and III., that in considering the deaths of nonresidents and those from outlying institutions, certain additions and deductions from the parishes have to be made in respect of themselves, but when considering the Borough as a whole, then from the 2779 deaths which were registered there must be deducted 460 deaths of actual nonresidents and 483 deaths in institutions entirely outlying must be added, making a total for the Borough of 2802 upon which the death rate is calculated. The 1008 deaths in Poplar included 18 illegitimate children, 10 male and 8 female; the 1120 deaths in Bromley included 19 illegitimate children, 6 male and 13 female; the 674 Bow deaths included 10 illegitimate children, 5 male and 5 female, making a total for the Borough of 47 deaths of illegitimate children. 7 8 Males. During the year 4380 notifications were received under the Notifications of Births Act, 1907. Discrepancies in Registration of Births. The Public Health and Housing Committee considered a report of the Medical Officer on the subject of discrepancies in the dates of births, as notified to the Medical Officer, and the same births as registered by the Registrars, and directed the attention of the Registrar-General be called to the matter. The following is the report mentioned above :— "An anomalous state of affairs has been revealed since the adoption of the Notification of Births Act, 1907, in the Borough. "By Section 1 (4) the Registrars of Births and Deaths shall at all reasonable times have access to notices of births received by the Medical Officer of Health under this Act or to any book in which those notices may be recorded. "By Section 2 (5) in London the Medical Officer of Health of every Metropolitan Borough in which this Act is in force shall send weekly to the London County Council, on a form prescribed by the Local Government Board, a list of all notices of births received by him under the Act during the week. "In Poplar, the Public Health Department, to assist the Registrars, sends a press copy of the weekly list mentioned above, to the Registrars of Births and Deaths. Every week the Registrars forward to the Public Health Department a return of births registered by them during the week. When the Registrars' returns are received the children are for the most part over six weeks old. "Now, it is found in many instances the date of the birth of the child as given by the person registering the birth differs from the date notified to the Medical Officer of Health. For example:—The birth of a female child was notified on the 13th January to the Medical Officer of Health as taking place on the 12th January; but in the Registrar's return for the week ended 12th March the date of the birth was entered as 25th January, and the Registrar informs the Medical Officer of Health the person registering such birth rigidly adhered to the date being the 25th January; hence the Registrar had no alternative but to enter in his return the 25th January. 14 "Again, in the return of births registered in the Sub-District of Bromley during the week ended 20th March, there is an entry of a male child having been born on the 4th February, whereas on the notification card received on the 28th January the date of birth is given as 26th January, and there can be no doubt that the child was born on this latter date and not upon the 4th February as registered. "Each of the three Registrars of the Borough informs the Medical Officer of Health that frequently the date given by the person registering the birth varies from that which is returned to him by the Medical Officer of Health. In some cases the Begistrars, by calling attention to the date which appeared on the notification of birth card supplied by the Public Health Department, is able to register the date previously given to the Medical Officer of Health, but in others the person registering the birth will not admit any mistake; hence the Registrar has no option but to enter the date which must obviously be incorrect, as there can be no doubt that the date on the notification of birth "card is the more correct, as it is posted within 36 hours after the birth. "The probable explanation is the parents give the wrong date of the births, having failed to register such births within six weeks, as required by the Registration Act, and thereby avoid the penalty. "A birth notified under the Notification of Births Act as happening out of wedlock on 31st January, was given the mother's name on the notification card to the Medical Officer of Health, and subsequently appeared in the Registrar's return for the week ended 13th March registered in the father's name, as if the child had been born in wedlock." There can be no doubt the adoption of the Notification of Births Act and the sending to the Registrars copies of the filled-in form which is sent weekly to the London County Council, assists the Registrars in registering the correct date of the birth. Medical Practitioners and the Notification of Births Act. Apparently all the births taking place in the Borough are not notified, for there is evidence that some medical practitioners do not comply with the provisions of the Notification of Births Act. Upon the adoption of the Act its provisions were brought to the notice of the medical practitioners and midwives practising within the 15 Borough by public posters, supplemented by circulars, which were sent out by post; and again under date 3rd February a circular letter was sent (by the direction of the Public Health and Housing Committee) to the medical practitioners and midwives, calling attention to their duties under the Notification of Births Act, 1907, and setting out the provisions of the Act. They were also informed "that the Council will, upon application, supply addressed and stamped envelopes for the purpose of notification" (a pattern was forwarded) and " that the Council will be obliged if you will kindly make known the provisions of the Act to anyone whom it may concern." A list of boundary streets and roads has been supplied to the medical practitioners and midwives, so that medical practitioners and midwives have received ample notice as to their statutory obligations. Upon these facts being brought to the notice of the Public Health and Housing Committee, the Committee were of opinion it is very desirable all births should be notified to the Medical Officer as provided by the Notification of Births Act, and regretted that some medical practitioners decline to carry out the provisions of that Act. The Committee reported the matter to the Council, and stated that in such cases legal proceedings would in future be taken against offenders where necessary. Stamped postcards are sent out to the medical practitioners and midwives instead of letter cards so that the postage is one halfpenny instead of one penny, which means a saving to the Borough. Health Visitor. London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1908—General Order of Local Government Board. The Local Government Board issued a General Order under the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1908, with respect to the qualifications, mode of appointment, duties, salary, and tenure of office of Health Visitors, who may be appointed by Metropolitan Borough Councils. The London County Council, by resolution, expressed the opinion that the status of any Health Visitor appointed under the Act should not be less than that of a female sanitary inspector employed by the Borough Council, and in determining the amount which it would contribute towards the salary of any Health Visitor, the London County Council would take into consideration the question whether the officer is paid a salary commensurate with the status of a female sanitary inspector. 16 Appointment of Health Visitor. In March, 1908, Miss A. E. Hall was appointed Health Visitor for the Borough. The appointment was made a temporary one, with a view to re-appointment under the provision of the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1908, then under consideration by Parliament, which enables the County Council to pay one-half the salaries of Health Visitors appointed by the Metropolitan Borough Councils. Whilst this report is being written the Local Government Board approved the selection of Miss Hall as Health Visitor to the Borough. The duties of the Council's Health Visitor are to visit births and give advice on feeding and management of infants, and on cleanliness and ventilation of homes, to visit cases of infantile summer diarrhœa: also to organise and direct voluntary* assistance in the work. PUBLIC HEALTH (TUBERCULOSIS) REGULATIONS, 1908. Total Number of Notifications; Poor Law Institutions Inside and outside Borough; Total Number of Poor Persons Notified; Disinfection of Rooms and Articles (see page 69); Streets with which casks of Pulmonary Tuberculosis (Poor Persons) were connected; Oaths Act, 1909; Gore Farm Hospital as a Sanatorium; Compulsory Notification and Practical Reforms; Tuberculous Milk, London County Council (General Powers) Bill, 1910 (see page 141.) Upon the coming into force of the Public Health (Tuberculosis) Regulations, 1908, the Public Health and Housing Committee reported to the Council that they had approved the issue, in connection with cases notified under the regulations, of cards containing instructions to patients and their friends as to precautions to be taken by them. The Committee also reported that they had approved the supply, where necessary, of spitting cups and bottles, on loan, for use in such cases. Special cards, deprecating the practice of spitting as a cause of spreading the infection of tuberculosis, were distributed throughout the district. Cards were also left at all the hair-cutting and shaving rooms, the eating-houses and coffee-shops, bakehouses, public-houses and a large number of factories. Notifications. Gore Farm Hospital as a Sanatorium. The Stepney Borough Council urged upon the Metropolitan Asylums Board the desirability of the utilisation of the Gore Farm Hospital, lying idle, as a sanatorium for consumptives, but the Board, who had the matter before them on several occasions, had come to the conclusion they are not in a position to act without the sanction and direction of the Local Government Board. The Stepney Borough Council therefore appealed to the Local Government Board to allow the Metropolitan Asylums Board to use their vacant Isolation Hospitals for the reception of consumptive patients for sanatorium treatment. The Poplar Borough Council on several occasions had urged the utilisation of the Gore Farm Hospital as a sanatorium for consumptives. The Public Health and Housing Committee recommended the Council to support the appeal of the Stepney Borough Council to the Local Government Board to allow the Metropolitan Asylums Board to use their vacant Isolation Hospitals for treatment of consumptive patients. 25 Compulsory Notification and Practical Reforms. The Public Health and Housing Committee considered a letter from the St. Marylebone Board of Guardians, expressing the opinion that the following policy is the one most likely, at present, to bring about good results in the fight against lung tuberculosis:— "I. Ideal aims for the whole nation. (a) Compulsory notification of all cases in England, and until this can be obtained, compulsory notification of cases in Hospitals and Dispensaries. (b) Compulsory removal and detention of cases, when, owing to neglected condition and the state of the disease, they constitute a danger to the community, unless the spread of the disease can be prevented at home. II. Practical reforms which could be begun at once. (a) The establishment in every Infirmary of special wards (where non-existent) for phthisis. (b) The formation, in connection with the Health Committees in every Borough, of a body of visitors, either voluntary or paid, who shall disseminate literature and preach unceasingly the need of cleanliness and fresh air. (c) That the Local Government Board be invited to set apart any Institutions, with suitable land attached, to which patients might be sent from the various Infirmaries when they are sufficiently recovered to undertake any light work, under medical supervision, but are not well enough to return home and enter the labour market." At present there is compulsory notification of pulmonary tuberculosis only in the cases of inmates of Poor Law Institutions or of persons in receipt of Poor Law relief or medical relief. The Committee were of opinion the adoption of the proposals of the St. Marylebone Board of Guardians is desirable, and recommended the Council to concur in this opinion, and the Local Government Board be so informed. Nomenclature of Diseases. Under the heading of " Diarrhoea" are included deaths registered as due to Epidemic diarrhoea, Epidemic enteritis, Infective enteritis, Zymotic enteritis, Summer diarrhoea, Dysentery and Dysenteric diarrhoea, Choleraic diarrhoea, Cholera (other than Asiatic or epidemic) and Cholera Nostras. Under the heading of "Enteritis are included deaths registered as due to Enteritis, Muco-enteritis, Gastro-enteritis, Gastric catarrh, and Gastro-intestinal catarrh. Under the headings of "Cancer" and "Puerperal fever" are included all registered deaths from causes comprised within these general terms. Thus: under "Cancer" are included deaths from Cancer, Carcinoma, Malignant disease, Scirrhus, Epithelioma, Sarcoma, Villous tumour, and Papilloma of bladder, Rodent ulcer. Under "Puerperal Fever" are included deaths from Pyaemia, Septicaemia, Sapræmia, Pelvic peritonitis, Peri- and Endo-Metritis occurring in the Puerperium. Under "Congenital Defects" are included deaths from Atelectasis, Icterus neonatorum, Navel haemorrhage, Malformations and Congenital hydrocephalus. Under "Tuberculous Meningitis" are included deaths from Acute hydrocephalus. Under "Other Tuberculous Diseases" are included deaths from Tuberculosis, Tuberculosis of bones, joints and other organs, Lupus and Scrofula. * Tables VIII., IX., X., XI. gastritis is a separate disease. 36 TABLE VIII. Notifications; Institutions; Workmen's Compensation Act and Dangerous Infectious Diseases—Proposed Inclusion; Errors in Diagnosis; Bacteriological Examinations; Infectious Diseases in Milk Shops and on Homeworkers' Premises; Oaths Act, 1909. There were 481 notifications of infectious diseases in Poplar, 462 in Bromley, and 246 in Bow, making a total of 1189 for the Borough of Poplar; these do not include 66 second notifications. In the above figures no deductions have been made for cases removed to the Metropolitan Asylums Board's hospitals, which were certified as not having a notifiable disease. Of the 481 patients notified in Poplar, 390 were removed to hospitals and institutions; of the 462 in Bromley 366 were isolated, and of the 246 in Bow 173 were removed, so that of a total of 1,189 notifiable diseases in the Borough 929 patients had been removed from their homes.* Workmen's Compensation Act and Dangerous Infectious Diseases—proposed Inclusion. The attention of the Public Health and Housing Committee was called to cases of nurses and attendants at Isolation Hospitals, and others engaged in connection with dangerous infectious diseases, who had died in the course, and as a result of, the nature of their duties. These cases * See Tables xxiv.-xxvii. 56 are not covered by the Workmen's Compensation Act, although certain other diseases which may be incurred in industrial processes are so covered by reason of their inclusion in the third Schedule, under the provisions of Section 8 of the Act. Sub-section 6 of Section 8 empowers the Secretary of State to make orders for extending the provisions of the Section to other diseases and injuries due to the nature of any employment specified in the order, not being injuries by accident. It was considered the inclusion of dangerous infectious diseases would enable insurances to be effected without material increase of premium. The Committee were of opinion it is desirable the Workmen's Compensation Act should be made to apply to dangerous infectious diseases contracted in the course of employment in such cases, and recommended the Council—That the Secretary of State for the Home Department be urged to make an order for inclusion in the Third Schedule of the Workmen's Compensation Act, of infectious diseases notifiable under any Statute or Order, in the cases of persons employed in removal of infectious patients, disinfection of premises, clothing and effects, in connection with such infectious diseases, or in attendance at Isolation Hospitals. A communication was received from the Secretary of State for the Home Department, with reference to the resolution of the Council urging the extension of the Third Schedule of the VVorkmen's Compensation Act to infectious diseases contracted by persons employed in connection with Isolation Hospitals or in disinfection. The Home Secretary stated that the matter had been considered by the Departmental Committee on Compensation for Industrial Diseases, and they expressed the opinion that neither the wording of the Act, nor the Third Schedule in its present form, nor the discussion of the Bill in Parliament, give any indication that our legislation on Workmen's Compensation contemplates cases of ordinary illnesses contracted by chance through exposure to infection while in an employer's service. The Home Secretary endorsed the opinion of the Departmental Committee. Errors in Diagnosis. Diphtheria 10th Sept. do. — The corrected number of notifications would be— Poplar 481 — 23 = 458 or 7-53 per 1000 living Bromley 462 — 21 = 441 or 6-61 Bow ... ... 246 — 10 = 236 or 5-46 ,, Borough of Poplar 1189 — 54 = 1135 or 6'64 ,, 60 In the course of the year 1909 it will be seen no fewer than 54 patients or a percentage on the total admissions of 6.28 were, after admission at the fever hospitals, found not to be suffering from the diseases (notifiable) mentioned in the medical certificates upon which they were removed to hospital. The percentage on the total (admission) scarlet fever cases was 4.20, diphtheria cases 11.44, and enteric fever cases 25.92. Among the 30 cases wrongly certified as scarlet fever there were 2 broncho-pneumonia, 3 erythema, 9 tonsillitis, 1 measles, 1 rotheln, 1 varicella, 1 urethritis, 1 burns, and 2 cases were diphtheria, and not scarlet fever. Among the 25 cases wrongly certified as diphtheria were 7 of tonsillitis, 2 measles, 1 septic pharyngitis, 1 tonsillar abscess, 1 pharyngeal paralysis. 6 cases were scarlet fever and not diphtheria. Among the 7 cases of typhoid fever wrongly certified were 1 appendicitis, 2 lobar pneumonia, 1 meningitis, and 1 helminthiasis. Facilities are still afforded to practitioners to have swabbings from throats of doubtful cases of Diphtheria, and specimens of blood from doubtful cases of Typhoid Fever bacteriologically examined. (See pages 104 and 108.) Arrangements are still made that the positive results of bacteriological examinations are communicated to the medical officers of the Metropolitan Asylums Board's Fever Hospitals at the time when the removals of patients are telephoned or by letter afterwards if the result be not to hand at time of removal, or if the removal has not been effected through the Public Health Department. Infectious Diseases in Milkshops and Homeworkers' Premises. Three cases of scarlet fever, 1 of diphtheria, 2 of enteric fever and 2 of measles were connected with milkshops in Poplar. Two cases of scarlet fever, and one of measle3 were connected with milkshops in Bromley. Two cases of diphtheria, one of erysipelas, and one of measles, were connected with like premises in Bow. The sale of milk was stopped until after the premises and utensils had been disinfected.* Eight premises with outworkers were† connected with infectious cases notified, 2 in Poplar, 4 in Bromley, and 2 in Bow—6 scarlet fever, and 2 diphtheria. No notices were served under the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901, Sections 108 and 110, but, where there was any risk of infection the work was discontinued, and any work found on the premises was disinfected before being returned. One case of measles‡ (Bromley) was also connected with outworkers' premises, and where work was found on the premises it was disinfected before being returned if there were any likelihood of infection, but there is no power to prevent work being executed on outworkers' premises where measles exists. For notices which had been received from Port Sanitary Authorities respecting passengers coming to the Borough of Poplar see page 91. Oaths Act, 1909, see page 187. Work of the Plant during the Summer Period, 1909; Stability of Fluid; Output of Fluid; Distribution of Fluid; Cost of Material and Electricity; Condition of Plant; Total Quantity Manufactured since Installation of Plant and Monetary advantage to Borough; Total Cost of Electrolytic Fluid, Corks, Labels and Bottles, 1906-1909 (four years); Expenditure for the Years ended 31st March, 1907, 1908, 1909, and Half Year to September 30th, 1909. plus estimate for Half Year to March 31st, 1910, and Estimate for Year ending March 31st, 1911; Cost of First Plant; Installation of Second Plant, Cost, Advantages, output and strength of Fluid; Cost of Carbolic Disinfectants, etc., 1903, 1904, 1905; Suggested use of Electrolytic Fluid for sprinkling the floors of the Class Rooms of the London County Council Schools; Swimming Baths, the use therein of the Electrolytic Fluid. Work of the Plant during Summer Period 1909. At the seven depots during the Summer period the electrolytic fluid was in great demand by the public; so great was the demand that the entire requirements of the Works Department could not be met; no fluid could be given out for street watering and sewer manhole flushing, but was delivered for the street gullies. The requirements of the Institutions of the Guardians and the Managers of the Sick Asylums were not curtailed. To meet the public demand at the depots, overtime at the central manufacturing depot had to be resorted to, as at the end of each day all fluid had been distributed at the central depot. After a run of eight hours the top supply tank was replenished, the electrolysers washed out and a fresh start made. On some days there was an output of over 300 gallons. At the central depot alone during the hot weather in one day as many as eight hundred vessels of all descriptions were filled with the fluid to satisfy the public call. The demand at the seven depots was at one time so great that the depots had to be supplied with fluid on two occasions during the week in place of the usual once. 71 Stability of Fluid. During the intensely hot weather the stability of the fluid was good, which speaks well for the method employed. Bottles (37 gross 10 doz.) £28 3 9 Corks (150 gross) 7 10 0 Labels (40,000) 3 0 0 £38 13 9 Condition of Plant. After working for nearly four years and not having been cleaned for a period of over twelve months, the apparatus was taken to pieces, over, hauled and thoroughly cleaned. The platinum wire and slates upon which it is wound were found in perfect condition—these are the positive electrodes and are the costly portion of the plant. In the apparatus there are forty pairs of zinc plates—the negative electrodes—and experience shews that the zinc electrodes will last from four to five years. The cost of a pair of zinc electrodes with the necessary nuts and bolts is five shillings and sixpence. It may be mentioned that experience shews that by somewhat altering the shape of the zinc electrodes their lives may be considerably increased and give greater efficiency in the working of the plant. The Chief Disinfector supervised the working and cleansing of the plant, and also superintended the distribution of the fluid at the seven depots and the various Institutions of the Borough. Total Quantity Manufactured since Installation of Plant. Monetary Advantage to Borough. Since the plant has been installed, a period now reaching four years, nearly 90,000 gallons have been manufactured at a cost for electricity and materials of under £175. The great monetary advantage to the Borough since the installation of the Electrolytic Plant need not be referred to in detail, as the fact has been the subject of reports again and again. The Public Health Department is not only furnished with the disinfectant and the various institutions of che Council (the public baths, etc.), but the institutions of the Managers of the Sick Asylums and of the Board of Guardians (within and without the Borough) are supplied with an unlimited quantity free. 74 For the fluid supplied to the Works Department a charge of 1d. per gallon is made, the Public Health Department is credited with this amount although no actual monetary transaction takes place. As applicants now usually bring their own receptacles to the depots, there is also a very appreciable saving in the expenses on bottles alone. Cost Advantages. Output and Strength of Fluid. On account of the demand for the fluid, the dismantling, overhauling and cleansing of the apparatus had to be carried out on Sunday, the men working overtime; this, together with the fact that to meet the public demand overtime had to be resorted to and the supply to the Works Department curtailed, induced the Committee to consider the advisability of recommending the Council to purchase an additional plant. There was no need for new saturators. In view of the difficulty of meeting the demand for the fluid, and of the use of the disinfectant in connection with the Public Swimming Baths, the Public Health and Housing Committee were of opinion the duplication of the plant was necessary, and recommended the Council that additional plant, as specified, for manufacture of disinfecting fluid by Electrolysis, be obtained and fitted up at an estimated cost of £430. The new plant has been installed whilst this report is being written (February, 1910) and is as follows:— Four Electrolysers* good for an output of 25 gallons of fluid at a * The tanks were, made of earthenware (which if well glazed will never wear out). Earthenware is one of the best insulating materials. Tanks made of slate contain iron (it seems impossible to obtain slate free from iron) and were being constantly destroyed by the action of the electric current and had to be often replaced. POPLAR ELECTROLYTIC FLUID INSTALLATION. The additional plant is shown on the left. The wire guard protects against injury from the motor and wheels which work the stirrers in the carboys. The three dark slate tanks of the old plant seen on the right have now been replaced by earthenware tanks. The glass funnels with mixing receptacles beneath are for the substance assuring stability. Above on the platform is the tank containing the solution to be electrolysed. 77 strength of * 4 grammes of Chlorine per litre and suited for a 240 volt circuit. One Electric Motor (for a 240 volt circuit), arranged to hang vertically and driving two stirring machines. Two Stirring Machines running on ball bearings and fitted with suitable grooved pulleys. Two Ebonite Stirring Rods fitted to machines. A Constant Head Flow Regulating Tank, suitably coated with preservative paint. A Flow Regulating Cock with vernier. A Mixing Tank of latest pattern. A Switchboard mounted with 2 D.P. Switch good for 25 amps in cast-iron case. 4 S.P. Porcelain Tubular fuses; An Ampere meter; A Voltmeter; with provisions for the Council's meters. A Series Rheostat. By including (as mentioned above) a stirring motor of vertical pattern, two stirring machines and two stirrers—one working on the old plant and one on the new—the production is made practically automatic and enables both plants to be worked simultaneously, and necessitates no addition to the staff. The cost of the entire plant as specified was £430; the Council provided rough labour that was required in addition. The saturated solutions are now lifted into the large top supply tank by means of a pump instead of being carried up by hand in pails, a matter occupying ten minutes instead of an hour and a half. The two plants can deliver in eight hours 400 gallons of electrolytic * With a current of 25 amperes there is obtained over 6 grammes of chlorine per litre. This efficiency is no doubt due to the new shape of the electrodes and to the earthenware tanks. G 78 fluid at a strength of 4.5 to 6.1 grammes of Chlorine per litre. The quantity mentioned above can of course be increased or diminished by opening or shutting the valves, the strength varying in proportion to the number of amperes allowed by the rheostat to pass through the electrodes. The following are copies of letters sent to the London County Council (Education Department) under the directions of the Public Health and Housing Committee:— Council Offices, Poplar, 29th September, 1909. Sir,— The Public Health and Housing Committee have had under consideration the question of the possibility of the spread of infectious disease by means of dust raised in school rooms by the movements of the children, and they are advised this is a probable source of infection. On the 26th September, 1906, an offer was made to the London County Council by this Council, but not then accepted, to deliver, free of cost, supplies of disinfectant for use in the schools in the Borough. I am directed to suggest that, experimentally, supplies of the disinfectant should be accepted for use in sprinkling the floors in six selected schools in the Borough, and the effect on the condition of the children and their attendance noted. I am to add that the Public Health and Housing Committee will be prepared to recommend the Council to renew its offer of September, 1906, to supply the disinfectant, with instructions for use, free to all the schools in the Borough, should the County Council desire to continue its use. I am, Sir, Your obedient Servant, (Signed) LEONARD POTTS, Town Clerk. The Executive Officer, Education Offices, London County Council. 80 Council Offices, Poplar, 27th September, 1906. Sir,— Disinfectants. I am directed by the Public Health aud Housing Committee to offer supplies of Electrolysed Salt Water disinfectant manufactured by this Council, for use in the Public Elementary Schools in this Borough. 1 enclose papers describing the nature, power and use of the disinfectant. The Committee will be pleased to arrange for delivery to the Schools, with necessary instructions for use. It is not proposed to make any charge in connection with the supply. Yours faithfully, (Signed) LEONARD POTTS, Town Clerk. The Clerk, London County Council, Education Department, Victoria Embankment, W.C. The Electrolytic Fluid will neither stain nor discolour the floors and seats* of the class-rooms, for as well as disinfecting, it bleaches, which is an advantage over some other disinfectants. It is non-poisonous. At the time of writing this report the matter is receiving the attention of the Education Department of the London County Council. Swimming Baths—this use therein of Electrolytic fluid.† In September, 1909, with permission of Mr. Councillor Barge, the then Chairman of the Baths and Wash-houses Committee, experiments were made in order to determine if the water in * Its use is very essential for spraying the seats in the babies' class-rooms. † As this Report is going to press some adverse criticisms are appearing in the Public Press to the effect that the Council is adding the Electrolytic Fluid in order to save the use of fresh water. This statement is absolutely untrue; in fairness to the Public and to the Council I take this opportunity to deny it, and to state most definitely it is an absolute mis-statement founded on misrepresentation. The Council has not this parsimonious object in view in seeking to provide their bathing supporters with the most wholesome bathe possible Neither is the Council's object to disinfect the bathers, another misrepresentation. The fact is a swimming bath cannot be emptied, cleansed and refilled at all times, so the Council desires to keep the water clean, sweet and pure as far as possible for the afternoon bathers after it has been unavoidably polluted by those of the morning] 81 the swimming baths of the Borough could be kept pure, purified, or sterilised by means of the Electrolytic Fluid. Mr. Councillor Barge devoted a large amount of time to this most important matter. Mr. Councillor Barge was present at the experiments, and the then Chairman of the Public Health and Housing Committee was also present and took part in one of the experiments. For financial reasons water in public swimming baths cannot be constantly changed, and it is obvious that after a large number of persons have bathed in the same water, more especially persons who have no convenient and proper bathing arrangements in their own homes, the water must become charged with impurities, polluted with organic matter and permeated with bacteria— the organic matter from the bodies forming a good medium for the bacteria to feed upon and thereby multiply; moreover, it cannot be concealed that swimming baths may, without detection, be used by persons who are unconsciously or otherwise suffering from communicable disease or from such diseases as may have a tendency to further pollute, i.e., skin maladies. In the act of swimming, as in all other exercises, the skin helps to eliminate the waste matter accumulating in the blood due—so to speak —to the expenditure of the fuel which maintains the swimmer's energy. This waste matter with perspiration is also absorbed by the water. It is a well-known fact that the water in swimming baths remains clearer longer at the latter part than at the first part of the bathing season for the simple reason that skins of some of the habitual bathers become more and more cleansed, t * In order to give some idea of the size of the germs of disease, and the tremendous vital activity possessed by them, which disinfection aims at destroying, I will quote Cohn, who estimated that a single bacillus weighs about 0.000,000,000,024,243,672 of a grain; forty thousand millions, 1 grain; 289 billions, 1 pound. After twenty-four hours the descendants from a single bacillus would weigh of a grain; after two days, over a pound; after three days, sixteen and half million pounds, or 7,366 tons. Of course, these figures would apply if there were a continual supply of food, and no impediments—which, fortunately for us, are many—to the growth of such germs, fresh air and sunlight being our ohief protectors. "Let us assume," says Cohn, " that a microbe divides into two within an hour, then again into eight in the third hour, and so on. The number of microbes thus produced in twenty-four hours would exoeed sixteen and a half millions; in two days they would increase to forty-seven trillions; and in a week the number expressing them would be made up of fifty-one figures. At the end of twenty-four hours the microbes descended from a single individual would occupy 1/40th of a hollow cube, with edges 1/25th of an inch long, but at the end of the following day would fill a space of 27 cubic inches, and in less than five days their volume would equal that of the entire ocean." † At the public baths of the Borough for the sum of one penny a child can have a 82 There is no doubt that from the comparative roughness of the surface and the porous and uneven nature of the interstices between the glazed bricks lining the bath, numerous germs are harboured and the growth of these germs helps to render the bath water impure. The ordinary scrubbing and cleansing, however well applied, cannot be entirely successful in the dislodgement of the whole of this accumulation of bacteria, which makes it necessary to resort to some process of sterilization to render what may be remaining inert. The temperature of the bath water is usually kept at 72 degrees, and it must be remembered that for germs taking part in the ordinary processes of putrefaction the temperature of warm summer weather—68 to 76 degrees—may be taken as the average optimum. Varying in time according to the number of bathers, particularly in summer, within twenty-four hours changes—without doubt due to the rapid growth of bacteria—are noticed in the water of a swimming bath.* It assumes a darkish colour, and if left in the bath grows still darker; at the bottom of the bath it becomes somewhat slimy and there is a feeling of stickiness about the body after bathing. When the used water is drawn off this slime leaves the bottom of the bath in a slippery condition and consequently difficult to stand upon when scrubbing and cleansing is in progress; the slime also emits an offensive odour which has a tendency to produce nausea and sore throat. Slime with grease is also deposited on the tiles at the water level; this should be removed by washing with strong hot soda water when the bath is emptied. Mr. Councillor Barge, the then Chairman of the Baths and Washhouses Committee, who had closely followed the experiments daily, considered the baths should be emptied and re-filled with fresh water as usual. He was of opinion the object of the scheme should be not so much to save water, as to give the bathers a pure bath and prevent the spread of disease, by sterilising the water and interior surfaces of the bath, and oxidising, as far as possible, the organic substances that must private warm bath, and for a like sum men and women can obtain a private cold bath. For the sum of two pennies men and women can have a private warm bath. In the above cases one towel is provided, bathers can bring their own soap or purchase a piece at the bath for the sum of a half-penny. The use of a second towel can be obtained for one half-penny, or a rough Turkish towel for one penny. * In fact, even in hot weather, after a few hours, changes will be noticed in swimming bath water without any bathers polluting it. Without doubt these changes are due to the water remaining still in a covered building and in a receptacle which has not been sterilised. 83 necessarily be present having regard to the large number of persons using the bath. It was patent to observers that the water kept clear for a longer period and was more refreshing to bathers with the Electrolytic Fluid in it than without it. The opinion of masters and mistresses attending with the boys and girls from the schools was that the water, by the addition of the Electrolytic fluid, was fresher, and more invigorating, and had lost its body odour. The secretary of a swimming club on Tuesday night, 14th September, stated that the members felt much fresher, and had lost that " dead feeling" when coming out of the bath. A voluntary statement was made by one of the women employed in the laundry by the Council that the swimming costumes of the men and women have no unpleasant odour since the fluid had been used in the bath water; she stated that previously they gave off a most unpleasant odour. This was not to be wondered at having regard to the result of the bacteriological examination of a sample of the water reported upon to the Committee. After the bath had been run out, the Superintendent stated that there was an absence of the slime usually found at the deep end of the bath. The cleansing of the bottom and sides of the bath was much more easily accomplished. The water of a swimming bath becomes discoloured after being used by only a few bathers; as mentioned above the germs from the body multiply enormously (see foot-note, page 81), the warm temperature at which the bath water is kept being favourable to their growth and development; and it is noticed by the Attendants that it very soon gives off bad odours after a number of bathers have used it; therefore if anything can be done to arrest and destroy these growths it must be advantageous not only to the health of the users of the baths, but indirectly to the health of the public at large. A single contingent of bathers will pollute the water of a swimming bath, consequently, where there are a number of bathers the water should be renewed several times during the day, but as this cannot be done, it must be good to endeavour to keep the water sterile whilst the bathers are in it. 84 Bathing in a swimming bath is not like bathing in a pure flowing stream. Large out-door swimming baths, unless supplied with running water or unless the water is frequently changed and the bottom of the lake cleansed must be a source of danger to the bathers. It is obviously healthier to bath in water three or four days old which is kept pure by the addition of Electrolytic fluid, than to bathe in water which is polluted by the bathers after a few hours' use when the fluid is not added to the bath. It is undoubted folly to overlook the very obvious and insidious dangers of public swimming baths, for it cannot be concealed the contaminated water of swimming baths may be the cause of many communicable diseases. There is a strong probability that consumption may be contracted from the sputum* and emanations and discharges from the mucous membrane of the nose and mouth of the bathers, for it must be remembered it is a common occurrence for the bath water to enter the mouths (and is of course at once expelled into the bath) by at least a goodly number of bathers who neglect oral hygiene whereby portions of food accumulate between the teeth; particularly when they may have diseased and uncleansed teeth. It is possible that involuntary micturition may take place in the bath through the temperature of the water being lower than the body, thereby causing the kidneys to act more freely than usual. Again, in a common bath communicable diseases may be contracted from the washing of the external parts of the bathers bodies. Should a bather happen to have had Typhoid Fever and be a "carrier" of the germ, it is obvious how dangerous becomes his presence in a public swimming bath. Another discomforting fact is further presented that during the rainy weather mud finds ready access to the only too expansive openings in the boots of many children who use the baths, and here again is a filthy source of pollution. Care is taken by the attendants to see that all children with dirty feet wash them before entering the bath, but the cleansing is perfunctory, and all children cannot even be so treated for the reason that time prevents such a proceeding. Dust and dirt also get into the bath-water from the hair of the girls who • For years endeavours have been made to stop the ravages of the White Plague, the Local Government Board have issued Regulations dealing with Pulmonary Tuberculosis, the Oaths Act has come into operation, Acts of Parliament have been passed, and at the present time further legislation is proceeding to prevent consumption. County Councils and Local Authorities exhibit notices on highways, in public places and in public conveyances requesting persons not to spit, pointing out the dangers of the filthy practice, and drawing attention to the penalties for infringement of the bye-laws. In swimming baths spitting goes on to an enormous extent, and the sputum from one person may directly enter the mouth and stomach of another. The public shuts its eyes to this danger, and the reformer is dubbed a faddist when he mentions it. 85 cannot wash their heads so readily as the boys. It is an acknowledged fact that water in a swimming bath is always dirtier after use by girls than after being used by boys. And so on may be depicted the horrors of bathing in contaminated swimming-bath water, to say nothing of the livestock from the heads and bodies of dirty bathers.* It is satisfactory to know that the Baths Superintendents are fully alive to the horrors and dangers of the swimming baths, and they do all in their power to safeguard the public health, as from time to time they skim (by means of a scum cloth) the water, overflow it, and as far as possible remove sputum, other objectionable matter floating on the surface, and clear by means of a net on a long pole any substances seen at the bottom of the bath. A bacteriological examination of the water of a swimming bath which had been in use for three days showed that it was polluted with practically dilute sewage. Of the bacteria the bacillus Coli Communis was present in the greatest proportion. This bacillus is the chief organism present in the small intestine in normal conditions, and with many other bacteria it also inhabits the large intestine. No comment is necessary to account for the presence of this bacillus in the water—the reason must be only too obvious, † Habitual frequenters of swimming baths, in their enthusiasm, should not close their eyes and hoodwink themselves to these existing dangers, but should endeavour to assist to keep the water of swimming baths as sweet and wholesome as possible. "The nation's health is the nation's wealth." Some habitues of swimming baths clamour for the baths to be supplied daily with fresh water instead of adding Electrolytic Fluid, but do these captious persons realise the financial aspect of the case? Thousands of gallons of water at 6d. per 1,000 gallons, and about three tons of fuel for each of the four large baths to raise the temperature of the water. Emptying and filling a swimming bath every day means a large amount of labour, consequently extra hands would have to be employed at night when the bath is emptied, washed and refilled, and then there must not be lost sight of the time taken up in cleaning the dressing boxes. Without doubt the daily emptying and purifying of the swimming baths and refilling them with fresh water is an ideal state of affairs, but this * See Addendum at end of Report. † It is indeed strange that in these days of sanitation, thousands of school children —during a week—should bathe in slimy water containing Bacilli Coli Communis and sputum; wipe themselves with a towel and return to school. The Poplar Borough Council's action to prevent bathing in such polluted water should be upheld by the public, more especially as school-masters have stated that the boys smell fresher now than in previous years after coming from the baths. 86 cannot be carried out at all events in the Borough of Poplar, where as matters stand at the present time the baths are kept open at a great financial loss to the ratepayers. Anyone who respects his health and desires to enjoy in comfort a good wholesome clean bathe, let him far rather go to the Bow Baths in the Roman Boad, or to any of the other baths in the Borough, pay a few pence (one penny if a child and two pennies if an adult), than patronise in Victoria Park the free and unrestricted bathing open-air lake frequented by thousands of all sorts and conditions of human beings who must obviously pollute the water to such an extent that here no comment is necessary, for such a state of affairs tells its own tale, but some folk will go into any kind of water, never considering the danger of doing so, as long as they can obtain a swim. Where filtration and aeration is carried out in public baths, it is noticed the filters become covered with distinctly offensive slime (aforementioned), occasioning much and continued cleansing of the filters; and then again unless the bath is entirely emptied and cleansed slime still remains at the bottom. Moreover, most swimmers have a decided objection to bathing in filtered water which has been in use for a long period, as they know fully well that evacuations from the body have been absorbed by the bath water. The addition of Electrolytic Fluid, as made in Poplar, to the bath water has a decidedly effective action on impurities in the water, and to have swimming baths cleansed and refilled frequently with fresh water and Electrolytic Fluid is strongly to be recommended as a most efficient way to deal with the lurking dangers to which attention has been drawn.* The water being kept clear, through the addition of the fluid, the bottom of the bath is visible, so that the attendants are enabled, by means of a net at the end of a long pole, to remove masses of dark coloured, flocculent matter. These masses consist of hair from the heads of the girls and fluff from the bathing costumes, which become worked together by the movement of the water and settle to the bottom of the bath when the water becomes still. After girls have used a swimming bath, hairribbons can be seen and removed. It is an advantage to be able to see to remove the fluffy masses mentioned above, as, upon being disturbed by the bathers, the masses disperse in the water and may enter the mouths of swimmers and probably be swallowed. For cleansing the dressing boxes and the side paths of the baths the Electrolytic Fluid is certainly better than phenol preparations, for should 87 any of the fluid be splashed (which it is difficult to avoid) into the bath water it does not matter. To keep the water in the swimming baths pure and free from germs is an object to be desired, more especially when such an object can be effected, for a short time at all events, at a cost of a few shillings. The Superintendents of the Baths of the Borough must be commended for the great interest they evince in keeping the swimming baths and dressing boxes (where disease and dirt may lurk) thoroughly cleansed. A large amount of labour is expended in this work and much time is occupied late in the evening when the baths are closed to the public. Full reports upon the whole matter (general, chemical and bacteriological) were furnished to the Baths and Wash-houses Committee, and appear in the Minutes of the Council under date 30th September, 1909. The Baths and Washhouses Committee reported to the Council that they were satisfied with the results of the experiments in connection with the Swimming Baths at Poplar, and had authorised the use of the disinfecting fluid in the whole of the Council's Swimming Baths.* The * The electrolytic disinfectant manufactured at Poplar is practically a solution containing hypochlorite of magnesia, which has the advantage of being non-caustic, non-acid and non-poisonous. Other hypochlorites belong to the caustic and alkali and alkaline-like earth groups of substances. A hypochlorite of magnesia belongs to the magnesia group, and for commercial purposes can only be made cheaply by the aid of electricity. When the Electrolytic Fluid is added to water containing organic matter, a powerful oxidisation takes place and any bacteria present are killed, the active part of the sterilising fluid being used up, and when the fluid is added in its proper proportion there is left behind practically a very weak solution of sea water. Without doubt the great difficulty and anxiety will be fully appreciated which were experienced in the repeated trials and observations for determining the right quantity and strength of Electrolytic Fluid which should be added to the water of the swimming baths in order to hit the happy medium so as to keep the water pure and not to inconvenience the bathers. In the report to the Baths and Wash-houses Committee at their meeting of the 23rd September, 1909, it was suggested after laboratory experiments with water which had been in use for some days that— "Into the four baths, each containing over 70,000 gallons of water, 40 gallons "(4 carboys) of Electrolytic Fluid should be added when the baths are filled, and "be put in while the bath is being filled in order that the fluid and bath water "may be thoroughly mixed ; 20 gallons (2 carboys)should be added the next night, "and 20 gallons the third night, making 80 gallons (8 carboys) in all for each "filling. The fluid which is added after the first night should be emptied into the " water at as many points as possible so as to get it equally distributed, "† " Into the smaller, second-class, swimming bath at Poplar, containing 39,000 "gallons, on the night of filling 20 gallons (2 carboys) of Electrolytic Fluid should " be added, 10 gallons (1 carboy) on the second night, 10 gallons on the third night " (40 gallons in all, or 4 carboys), applied in the same manner as to the large baths. † It is a good plan to pour the fluid into a bucket from which it can be scattered into the bath water. 88 Committee also reported that they had appointed a Sub-Committee to confer with the Medical Officer as to the use of the disinfectant in the slipper baths "When the baths are newly filled the Electrolytic Fluid should always be "added during the filling, but the later additions should be governed by the " number of bathers and the condition of the water." No hard and fast rule is laid down respecting the later additions, each Bath Superintendent must use his discretion. If there have not been many bathers and the condition of the water remains good some days may elapse before the second addition is necessary, and the same remarks apply to the third addition. In practice these amounts were varied and for some months 40, 30 and '20 gallons for the large baths and 20, '20 and 10 gallons for the small bath were substituted, but whilst this report is being written the quantities have again been altered to 30, 20 and 20 gallons for the three large baths of over 80,000 gallons, 20, 20 and 20 gallons for the large bath in Poplar holding 75,000 gallons and 10, 10 and 10 gallons to the small Poplar bath, to see if these smaller additions would bring about beneficial results. The fluid must be well mixed and evenly distributed in the bath water. The strength of the fluid is now kep as near as possible at 2.5* grammes of chlorine per litre. In the first trials and for some considerable time afterwards the strength of the fluid was 4.2 to 4.5 grammes of chlorine per litre, but it was from time to time reduced until it reached that mentioned above. After a number of bathers have used the same water and polluted it a slight chlorous odour is noticed in the atmosphere of the baths (instead of the usual sickening "body smell"), due to the Electrolytic Fluid working upon the organic matter- oxidisation is taking place and chlorine compounds, most powerful germicides and deodorants are being given off. So long as this slight chlorous smell*—which might be really termed the "indicator"—is noticed, the Electrolytic Fluid is active, oxidising the organic matter, and either killing the germs or inhibiting their growth. It is obvious that swimming bath water which has had Electrolytic Fluid added to it previous to being used and has given off a chlorous odour after a number of bathers (say 1,500, for this number of persons may use the same water in the large baths of the district) have polluted the water and ceases to give off this faint chlorous odour, either more Electrolytic Fluid should be added or the water should be discharged into the sewers. For reasons which need not be entered into the latter course should be adopted. Ventilation is a most important item in maintaining the hygiene of a swimming bath. It is necessary in a confined building to have good constant ventilation to remove the sickening "body smell" due to a number of human beings undergoing exercise. Efficient ventilation is not only essential for health but will help to preserve the water. As already staled on page 83 it is noticed by the Attendants that it very soon gives off bad odours after a number of bathers have used it. When a solution of Electrolytic Fluid is used for washing out the dressing boxes and cleansing the bath generally, the "body smell" is not so much noticed, but good ventilation is necessary so that the faint chlorous smell may be carried away. The bath should not only be well ventilated during the day but as much as possible throughout the night, for when a large body of polluted water has had added to it Electrolytic Fluid faint vapour will rise due to the process of oxidisation going on in the water. The capacity of each of the five Baths in the Borough is as follows:— Poplar: 1st Class 75,000 gallons 2nd Class 39,000 „ Bow: 1st Class 85,000 „ 2nd Class 82,000 „ Isle of Cogs 82,000 „ 363,000 „ * Sufficient to give about 1 part available chlorine to 2 millions of water, † A well-known analyst states that this chlorous smell is due to chlorine compounds acting upon organic matter—saliva, perspiration, filth, etc. To face page 88 Annual Report, 1909. Swimming Baths—The Use therein of Electrolytic Fluid. It is very questionable whether the slight chlorous smell (mentioned on page 88) can now be detected because the strength of the fluid is kept at l.5 and 2.0 grammes of chlorine per litre for the Poplar and other baths respectively, as this strength from the appearance and condition of the bath water appears to be sufficient—for the time being at all events—either to kill the bacteria, or to inhibit their growth. The same quantities are added as mentioned on page 88, and the chlorine per 100 gallons, of the bath water is 3.38 grains (large bath) and 3.48 grains (small bath) for each addition in Poplar: 5.1, 3.4, and 3.4 grains of chlorine per 100 gallons of bath water for the Bow and Island Baths. CLEANSING OF PERSONS ACT, 1897.* The Council is empowered by the Cleansing of Persons Act, 1897, to give any person applying on the ground that he is infested with vermin, free use of any apparatus for cleansing his person and clothing, and may expend any reasonable sum on buildings, appliances and attendants for this purpose. Provision was made in 1905 for cleansing verminous persons. During the year 647 persons had applied for baths and for their clothes to be disinfested. Since the notices were posted throughout the Borough a total of 3,570 persons had applied for baths, etc., as mentioned above. Proposed New Shelter and Disinfesting (Bathing) Accommodation. The Works Committee reported to the Council that in view of the completion of the new Dust Destructor, it was desirable the Infectious Diseases Shelter† at Bromley Depot should be discontinued there in order that the cottage forming part of the shelter might be available for the Destructor foreman, who should live on the spot to ensure the efficient working of the Destructor plant, and it was also advisable the workshop forming the remainder of the present temporary shelter should be freed for use by the Works Department. It was suggested that the Council's former depot in Old Ford Road might be utilised for erection of a shelter, and in order that a scheme might be prepared and considered the Borough Surveyor communicated with the London County Council with a view to sanction being obtained for erection of the shelter on this site if so decided. The estimated cost was £2,650. *Whilst this report is being written the Public Health and Housing Committee reported to the Council they bad directed that the London County Council Education Committee be informed that the exclusive use of baths at the existing Cleansing Station will be available between 9 a.m. and 12 noon for the cleansing, under the London County Council (General Powers) Act, and the Children Act, of 12 verminous school children, at a fee of 2s. for each bath, including disinfection of clothing. The Council is bound under Section 60 (4) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, to provide temporary shelter for members of any family in which any dangerous infectious disease has appeared, who have been compelled to leave their dwellings for the purposo of enabling such dwellings to be disinfected by the Council. During the year one scarlet fever contact had used the shelter. 91 The London County Council was prepared to sanction the building, subject to conditions as to building, and to the dedication of a narrow strip of land to be thrown into Old Ford Road. These conditions were embodied in a document required to be sealed by the Council. The sealing was agreed to by the Council, but it did not commit the Council to proceed with the scheme, which was not carried out as it was considered the position was not sufficiently central. Whilst this report is being written the Public Health and Housing Committee had under consideration the present provision of shelter accommodation and of bathing and disinfesting facilities. In view of the desire of the Works Committee to obtain possession of the premises at present in use, the Public Health and Housing Committee were of opinion arrangements should be made to provide new shelter accommodation, including means for isolation of contacts during epidemics of infectious disease: also that accommodation should be provided on the same site for cleansing verminous persons and disinfesting their clothing, with sufficient suitable provision for treatment of verminous children sent from the Schools, under agreement with the London County Council. The Committee considered the most economical and convenient arrangement would be to provide the necessary accommodation on a site adjacent to the present disinfecting station, in a form involving as moderate a capital cost as possible, and the site adjoining the stone yard in Glaucus Street appeared to be the most suitable. The Public Health and Housing Committee recommended the Council that, subject to the consent of the London County Council being obtained to the proposed buildings, the vacant land adjoining the stone yard in Glaucus Street be rented and a Shelter and Disinfesting House be erected thereon, in accordance with the plan and specification of the Borough Surveyor, at an estimated cost of £1,260. Extension of Section 55 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891; Case of Anthrax in Man; London (Notification of Glanders) Order, 1907 ; Case of Anthrax in a Mare. In 1908 the Public Health and Housing Committee considered a communication from the Local Government Board on the subject of representations made by the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries as to the desirability, with a view to checking the spread of such diseases, of information being afforded by the Medical Officer of Health to the Clerk to the Local Authority under the Diseases of Animals Act, 1894, of cases or suspected cases of glanders, anthrax and hydrophobia in man. In order to afford this information the Local Government Board stated it is necessary a means of notification should be adopted, and the Board would be prepared to favourably consider an application for their approval of a resolution of the Council for extending to the diseases in question the provisions of Section 55 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891. Fourteen days' notice to members of the Council as required by Section 55 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, was given on the 22nd December, 1908. The London County Council gave notice that at a meeting held on the 23rd day of March, 1909, it made an " Order " that Section 55 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, with respect to the notification of infectious disease shall apply in the Administrative County of London to the diseases known as Glanders, Anthrax and Hydrophobia in Man. The Order was approved by the Local Government Board and came into operation on the 26th April. A Case of Anthrax in Man. In the months of July and September, 1909, samples of the urine and stools of the members of the family who had recovered from the fever, as well as some members who had not bee attacked, were examined at the Lister Institute with negative results. 104 54 cases of Typhoid Fever were notified during the year, but in many instances no causes or predisposing influences were stated. Errors in Diagnosis. Seven cases sent to various hospitals, 5 in Poplar, 1 in Bromley; and 1 in Bow were certified (see list pages 57-59) as not suffering from Typhoid Fever:—so that in the 54 notified cases, 7 patients were certified from hospitals of the Metropolitan Asylums Board* as not suffering from the complaint (see page 60). Bacteriological Examinations of Doubtful and Convalescent Cases. Forty specimens of blood from doubtful cases of Typhoid Fever were forwarded to the Lister Institute of Preventive Medicine. In 14 instances (35.0 per cent.) the Typhoid (Widal) reaction was obtained. On 22nd January, 1909, in accordance with arrangements made by the Local Government Board, directions were received from the Lister Institute of Preventive Medicine, with reference to the examination of the stools and urine of Enteric Fever patients discharged from the Metropolitan Asylums Board's Hospitals in cases in which the bacillus was still harboured. The arrangements have been carried out as far as practicable. On 1st January, 1910, instructions were received from the Local Government Board, extending the afore-mentioned arrangements to all cases of Typhoid Fever discharged from the Metropolitan Asylums Board's Hospitals, whether the bacillus was harboured or not upon the discharge of the patient from hospital. Deaths. Eight Poplar parishioners died from Typhoid Fever, 4 fatal cases occurred among the Bromley patients, and 2 among the Bow, making a total of 14 deaths in 54 cases. * Twenty-seven eases were removed to hospitals of the Metropolitan Asylums Board, and 23 cases were treated in other institutions. Bacteriological Examinations. 124 swabbings from throats of doubtful cases of Diphtheria were forwarded for bacteriological examination to the Lister Institute of Preventive Medicine.* The Bacillus Diphtheriæ was isolated in 35 instances (28.22 per cent.), and the Pseudo Diphtheritic Bacillus in 16 cases. On 9th December, 1909, a letter was received from the Lister Institute of Preventive Medicine with reference to the transmission of reports on the results of bacteriological examinations for the Bacillus of Diphtheria and therein it was stated as follows:— "All specimens from suspected cases of Diphtheria in your district received before 10 a.m. on week-days (Saturdays excepted), will be reported on if Positive the same evening between 5.30 and 6 p.m. by telegram, telephone or post, either to you, or to the sender of the specimen, as you may prefer. If the result is Negative no report will be sent until confirmed by the next morning's examination. "All specimens received at any time on Saturdays up to 10 p.m. will be reported upon by telegram or telephone to the senders of the specimens on Sunday forenoons if required, and the results will be confirmed by post to you on Mondays, but it is requested that the Institute may be informed whether your Council desire reports on Sundays and will authorise the cost of telegrams when it is not possible to telephone." The old arrangement was that, providing the specimen was received at the Lister Institute before 6 p.m., the result of the examination was forwarded to the Public Health Offices at about noon the following day, but if the specimen were received at the Institute either on Saturday or later than 6 p.m. on any other week-day, a day elapsed before the result of the examination was known at these Offices. On 10th December, 1909, the Assistant Secretary of the Lister Institute of Preventive Medicine, was written to as follows:— "The Caretaker of these Offices understands the transmission * See page 61. 109 of the reports to the Medical Practitioners and is always here, therefore I should be pleased to receive reports by telegrams addressed to me at these Offices as heretofore and also on Sundays (forenoon)." It is preferable to have the result in writing (telegram) as messages sent through the telephone are liable to be misunderstood. This new arrangement will hasten the removal to hospital or isolation at home of patients suffering from Diphtheria. Errors in Diagnosis. See pages 57-59 as to cases of Diphtheria admitted into Metropolitan Asylums Board's Hospitals which were certified as not having the complaint. No deductions have been made in respect of these patients. Condition of Housk Drainage. The 82 cases in Poplar were notified from 67 premises, the 85 cases in Bromley from 57 premises, and the 43 cases in Bow from 32 bouses, and of these premises the drains were found to be defective in 15 instances —7 Poplar, 7 Bromley, and 1 Bow. Isolation. 64 cases in Poplar (sub-district), 69 in Bromley, and 33 in Bow were removed to hospitals of the Metropolitan Asylums Board. Two patients from Poplar (sub-district) were treated in the East London Hospital for Children, one patient from Bromley was treated in St. Bartholomew's Hospital, and one patient from Bow was treated in the Sick Asylum. So that 170 patients out of 210 notified cases in the Borough were isolated. 110 WHOOPING COUGH. Deaths. Poplar. Bromley. Bow. Borough of Poplar. 1893 12 20 8 40 1894 58 95 57 210 1895 82 105 25 212 1896 49 32 62 143 1897 33 35 12 80 1898 34 53 38 125 1899 39 44 17 100 1900 25 28 20 73 1901 26 38 25 89 1902 31 27 10 68 1903 53 88 20 161 1904 30 46 14 90 1905 34 29 16 79 1906 33 53 15 101 1907 25 55 14 94 1908 25 31 24 80 1909 59 54 39 152 Fifty-eight deaths (thirty-six of which belonged to the Borough of Poplar) occurred in the Sick Asylum. For death rates see Tables XVIII. and XIX., page 47. At the end of the year there were 807 entries in the Measles Register {334 Poplar, 296 Bromley, and 177 Bow), representing the number of premises where measles had been stated to have occurred. Upon enquiry respecting these entries 21 cases were stated to have recovered, 5 were found not to be cases, 4 were stated to be suffering from a different disease other than the one notified, 1 wrong address was given, and 8 had moved away before enquiries could be made. So that practically as far as is known 789 cases of measles had occurred in the Borough. 112 Schools. On 20th February communication was received from the Medical Officer (Education) of the London County Council, that all unprotected children would be excluded from Class-room G of the Infants Department of Glengall Road School, from the 18th to the 22nd instant, on account of Measles. In acknowledging receipt of the letter the question was asked whether the Medical Officer (Education) wished disinfection carried out. On 2nd and 12th February information was received from the Medical Officer (Education) of the London County Council, concerning the exclusion from Millwall School and Monteith Road School, respectively, on account of Measles, of all unprotected children in, in the first case, the Infants' Department and, in the second case, Class-room E. of the Infants' Department. In acknowledging the receipt of the communications, the Medical Officer (Education) was asked whether he wished disinfection to be carried out at the Schools, and on 15th February he replied, as regards Monteith Road School, that he did not think any disinfection of Class room E. was necessary. On 20th March a letter dated the 19th March was received from the Medical Officer (Education) of the London County Council that instructions had been given for the exclusion from the 23rd to the 29th March inclusive of all unprotected children in Class-room C. of the Infants' Department of Malmesbury Road School, on account of the prevalence of Measles. In acknowledging the receipt of the communication the Medical Officer (Education) was asked whether he wished for disinfection to be carried out by the Public Health Department. On 23rd March a further letter was received from the Medical Officer (Education) stating that the period of exclusion of children from the above Class-room had been extended to the 2nd April, and that he did not think it necessary to disinfect the Class-room. On 28th April a letter was received from the Medical Officer (Education) London County Council, stating that instructions had been given to exclude, from 3rd to 7th May inclusive, all unprotected children in Classroom F. of the Infants' Department of Atley Road School, on account of Measles. The Medical Officer (Education) stated in a letter, dated 30th April, that he did not think it necessary to disinfect the Class-room. On 11th May two letters were received from the Medical Officer 113 (Education), London County Council, that instructions had been given to exclude until the 17th May (inclusive) all unprotected children from Classrooms C. and E. of the Infants' Departments of Alton Street and "Cubitt Town " Schools respectively on account of Measles. On 12th May a communication was received from the Medical Officer (Education) London County Council, stating that instructions had been given for the exclusion from 12th to 21st May (inclusive) of all unprotected children (Infants' Department) in Class-room C., and also to extend to 20th May the exclusion of children (Infants' Department) from Class-room E., of " Cubitt Town " School on account of Measles. On 12th May the Medical Officer (Education), London County Council, stated that it was unnecessary to disinfect Class-rooms of the Infants' Departments of the Alton Street and " Cubitt Town " Schools, for when a Class-room is closed on account of Measles, the school-keeper is instructed to thoroughly cleanse the room. Respecting outworkers and the articles disinfected by the Council, see pages 61 and 69. SPOTTED FEVER. EPIDEMIC CEREBRO-SPINAL MENINGITIS. The Local Government Board on 1st March, 1909, approved an Order of the London County Council requiring the notification of cases of cerebrospinal fever for a further period of twelve months from and including 13th March, 1909. Eight cases occurred within the Borough, 1 Poplar, 2 Bromley, and 5 Bow. Pour of the patients died,f shown as follow:— (1) A case of epidemic cerebro-spinal meiningitis (Spotted Fever) was notified on the 25th January as occurring in a male, aged 15 months, a resident of Bow. * Posterior basal meningitis is included in the term "cerebro-spinal fever," but does not include meningitis due to tuberculosis, syphilis, middle ear disease, and injury. The Local Government Board on 1st March, 1910, approved an Order of the London County Council requiring the notification of cases of Cerebro-Spinal Fever (Epidemi o Cerebro-Spinal Meningitis) for a further period of twelve calendar months from and including 13th March, 1910. † One death primary cause was certified as Measles. The other was certified as Tuberculous Meningitis, so that only two deaths from Spotted Fever appear in the statistical tables. 116 (2) Female, aged 4 years, of Old Ford Road, Bow, notified on 26th March ; admitted into Guy's Hospital, 18th March. The patient died in the East London Hospital for Children, Shadwell, on 27th May. The disease was complicated with measles. (7) On 13th July notification was received of a case of epidemic cerebro-spinal meningitis (spotted fever) occurring in a male aged 2¼ years, of Lefevre Road, Bow. The patient was admitted on 4th July into the East London Hospital for Children and died on 10th July. Death certified as being due to tuberculous meningitis.
