Trade Boards Act, 1909. (seh: page186). Proposed Abolition of Child Labour. Before the Labour Exchanges Act, 1909, became law the Council, upon the recommendation of the General Purposes Committee, passed a resolution urging upon His Majesty's Government the promotion of legislation for the abolition of child labour, whether half time or otherwise, up to 16 years of age. A special form is submitted by the. Secretary of State for the guidance of Medical Officers of Health (see pages 130 to 133) in preparing the report on factories and workshops in pursuance of Section 132 of the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901. In order that the form may be filled in without any difficulty from year to year, the Sanitary Inspectors have been urgently requested to give full and complete descriptions of the premises visited where any work is carried on, and to classify such premises in their note books before handing them to the clerks far the particulars to be entered in the journals, registers and index files. It can be easily understood that the classification of premises where work is executed will vary from time to time, according to the pressure of work or the nature of the work carried on—outworkers' premises may become domestic workshops, and also workshops,* and even factories; workshops may become factories; and workplaces may become workshops or factories, between the visits of the Sanitary Inspectors. * To wit, in Table A, under "Laundries, Domestic Workshops," there were 18 on the register at the end of the year, but during the year, especially in the summer, some of these premises were "Workshop Laundries," and the inspections would come under Workshop Laundries. 127 TABLE A. London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1908, Section 9—Vendors of Fried Fish, Fish Curers, and Rag and Bone Dealers—Proposed Bye-laws. The premises where offensive trades may be carried on within the Borough of Poplar are:— Yard attached to 39, White Post Lane Tripe Boilers. 277, Roman Road East London Soap Works Soap Boilers. 241, Old Ford Road Fat Melters. 80, White Post Lane 31, White Post Lane Messrs. H. Webb, Yeo Street Tallow melters and bone boilers. The interior of premises, 80, White Post Lane, were entirely reconstructed. Amended plans were received by the London County Council from the Sheppy Glue and Chemical Co. London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1908, Section 9.—Vendors of Fried Fish, Fish Curers and Rag and Bone Dealers.—Proposed Bye-Laws. The London County Council submitted draft Bye-laws relating to the conduct of the businesses of a Vendor of Fried Fish, Fish Curer and a Rag and Bone Dealer as follow:— Fried Fish Vendor and Fish Curer. Present vendors or curers are to give notice to the London County Council and Borough Council of name of owner, and address at which business is carried on. Vendors and curers newly established are to give notice before making alterations to premises, fittings or apparatus, and to furnish plans if required. There are also provisions for enforcing cleanliness, and prohibiting nuisance from effluvium or smoke; also for regulating structure and 139 fittings in newly established businesses. Yards used are to be properly paved and drained. Rag and Bone Dealer. Notice of name of owner, and address of business to be given to the London County Council and Borough Council. Rags, refuse or other materials from dust carts, dust wharf, or refuse heap not to be allowed on premises. Bones and fat to be removed at least twice a week from May to October (inclusive), and once a week during remainder of year. Rags, bones, fat, skins, etc., are to be stored or manipulated in a properly constructed room as specified. There are also provisions for enforcing cleanliness and proper repair of premises. The London County Council was prepared to consider representations from Borough Councils as to amending or altering the Bye-laws. The Public Health and Housing Committee were of opinion the Bye-laws are reasonable and necessary, and recommended the Council— That the draft Bye-laws submitted by the London County Council, with reference to the conduct of the businesses of a vendor of fried fish, a fish curer, and a rag and bone dealer, be approved. Part II. Sanitary Provisions. Power to Sanitary Authorities to remove from or refuse to enter on Register Names of Dairymen in certain circumstances. No circumstance arose during the year calling for the enforcement of the power given to the Sanitary Authority under the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1908, Section (5). See Sanitary Inspectors' reports, pages 199, 204, 209, 218, 223 and 239. London County Council (General Powers) Bill, 1910. Part VI. Milk Supply. Unwholesome Milk. Under Sections 33 to 36, the London County Council's Medical Officer or a deputy may take at any railway premises or other place in the County, samples of milk in transit, and if found to contain matter rendering it unwholesome, the sender is liable to penalties; and similar powers are conferred on Borough Councils against vendors in respect of milk offered for sale (other than on railway premises) in their own areas. L 142 Section 37 enables the London County Council to prohibit supply within the County from any dairy outside the County from which unwholesome milk has been supplied, and also to inspect such dairies. Tuberculous Milk. Section 42 enables the London County Council to inspect dairies and cows within the County (and outside the County on a Justice's Order) from which tuberculous milk is suspected. The provisions of the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1907, with respect to cows suffering from tuberculosis of the udder, and to the milk from such cows, are extended to cows suffering from any form of tuberculosis or emaciation. (Similar provisions contained in the London County Council (General Powers) Bill, 1908 and 1909, were approved by the Council, but were not proceeded with.)* * In the House of Commons on 7th April, 1910. the London County Council (General Powers) Bill was on the notice paper for second reading. Part VI. contained provisions for dealing with the Milk Supply. On the motion for the second reading of the Bill, Mr. Courthope moved its rejection as an amendment. He admitted that milk legislation was necessary, but he objected to the piece-meal proposals of the Bill. After the amendment had been seconded by Mr. Stanier, Mr. Walter Guinness, who spoke on behalf of the London County Council, said that, that Body would have preferred general legislation. After Mr. Walter Guinness had finished his speech, Mr. Emmott, Chairman of Ways and Means, asked the House to pass the second reading, and to take a division with regard to the milk supply, on the motion that it should be an instruction to the Select Committee to delete from the Bill the clauses relating to this subject. Mr. Courthope assented to this course. The Bill was read a second time without a division. Mr. Courthope then moved that it should be an instruction to the Committee to delete Part vi. (Milk Supply). After further speeches by members, among whom were Mr. Burns (President of the Local Government Board) and Mr. Walter Long, a division was taken, and ther voted— For the Motion 85 Against the Motion 81 Majority for the Motion 4 It was therefore ordered that it be an instruction to the Committee on the Bill to delete Part VI. Conference convened by the London County Council and proposed Amendment of the Law; London County Council (General Powers) Bill, 1910, Part IV.— Smoke Nuisance. 61 smoke nuisances were dealt with during the year:—12 Bow East, 4 Bow West, 14 North Bromley, 7 Bromley Central, 7 East combined, 8 West combined, and 9 Poplar South. 41 intimations and 25 statutory notices were served. No summonses were applied for. Conference convened by the London County Council and proposed Amendment of Law. At a Conference convened by the London County Council on the question of obtaining an amendment of the law with a view to dealing more effectively with nuisance from smoke, the following proposed amendments were approved, viz.:— Resolved—That Section 24 (b) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, which provides that "any chimney (not being the chimney of a private dwelling house) sending forth black smoke in such quantities as to be a nuisance," shall be a nuisance liable to be dealt with summarily under the Act, should be amended by the deletion of the word "black." 144 Resolved—That the word "chimney" in Section 24 (b) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, should be deemed to include— (i.) Openings through which smoke is emitted from buildings or places in which processes of manufacture are carried on, and the chimneys of any building or place where furnaces are used in operations carried on under statutory powers. (ii.) The chimneys of any Government workshop or factory. Resolved—That in special cases of nuisance arising under Sections 23 and 24 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, the proceedings in respect of any nuisance may, at the request of, and by agreement with the Sanitary Authority, be taken by the London County Council in such special cases. The Public Health and Housing Committee recommended the Council that the recommendations of the Conference be approved and supported. London County Council (General Powers) Bill, 1910. Part IV.—Smoke Nuisance. Section 20 amends the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, by deleting the word "black" in Section 24, under which any chimney (other than of a dwelling house) sending forth black smoke in such quantity as to be a nuisance may be dealt with by prosecution of the persons responsible. Section 24 is also extended to include factories owned or used by the Crown and places in which any process is carried on under statutory powers, and the word "chimney" is to include any opening through which smoke is emitted. Section 21 enables the London County Council, at the request of the Borough Council, to enforce the provisions of Section 23 of the Public Health (London) Act, requiring that furnaces and steam vessels shall consume their own smoke, and gives power to the London County Council to enforce the provisions of the Public Health (London) Act, with reference to smoke nuisance in cases where cause of nuisance arises outside the county and in cases where it arises on premises of a Metropolitan Borough Council. 145 FOOD AND DRUGS ACTS Result of Analyses; Legal Proceedings; Preservatives in Butter; Proposed Issuing of Regulations under Section 4 of the Sale of Food and Drugs Act, 1899, with regard to Cheese; Proposed Amendment of Sale of Food and Drugs Acts as to Warranties in the case of Milk. The following letter was submitted to the Public Health and Housing Committee:— Battersea Borough Council, Town Hall, Battersea, S.W., 29th December, 1909. Dear Sir, My Council have had under consideration the question of the sale of cheese deficient in fat, and have recently caused samples to be taken and submitted to the Public Analyst for analysis. Some of the samples in question have been certified to be deficient in fat to a considerable extent, and it is felt that proceedings should be instituted against the vendors. The Council are, however, faced by the difficulty that there is at present no official standard fixing a minimum limit for the quantity of fat which cheeses of different kinds should contain. In view of the circumstances, it appears to the Council to be useless to take proceedings in such cases as it is very doubtful whether the Council would obtain convictions in the absence of official standards or minimum limits such as those referred to. I have, therefore, by direction of the Council, addressed a letter to the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries drawing their attention to the matter and asking that the Board will be good enough to consider as to issuing Regulations under Section 4 of the Sale of Food and Drugs Act, 1899, with regard to cheese. In the event of your Council concurring I am directed to ask that they will kindly support the action of my Council in the matter. Yours faithfully, W. MARCUS WILKINS, Town Clerk. The Town Clerk, Poplar. 151 The Public Health and Housing Committee recommended the Council that the application of the Battersea Borough Council to the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries to issue Regulations under Section 4 of the Sale of Food and Drugs Act, 1899, with regard to cheese, be supported. Proposal as to Provision for enforcing Cleanliness in cases of Barrows and Stalls on which Food is Exposed or Deposited for Sale in the Streets and other places. The following letter was submitted to the Public Health and Housing Committee: — London County Council, County Hall, Spring Gardens, S.W. 28th June, 1909. Sir, Section 8 of the London County Council (General Powers) 154 Act, 1908, regulates the sanitary condition of premises on which food is sold or prepared for sale, and premises (such as yards, stables and arches) where is stored food to be sold by costermongers on barrows, can be dealt with under this Section, but it does not apply to barrows and other vehicles on which food is exposed for sale. A large amount of foodstuff is sold in London from barrows and stalls, and it appears to a Committee of the Council that some control should be exercised over their sanitary condition. This could be effected if legislation were obtained enabling sanitary authorities to ensure the observation of proper sanitary regulations in regard to articles of food exposed or deposited for sale in the streets and other places to which the provisions of Section 8 of the Act of 1908 do not apply. Before proceeding further in the direction of promoting legislation the Committee would be glad to know whether the several Metropolitan Borough Councils are in favour of the proposed extension of the authority given by Section 8 of the Act of 1908, and they would welcome any suggestions as to the best methods of regulating the sanitary condition of barrows and stalls. I have been directed to ask that the observations of the Borough Councils may be submitted not later than 8th July, 1909, in order that proposals may be submitted to the Council in time for the promotion of legislation in the next session of Parliament. I am, Sir, Your obedient Servant, G. L. GOMME, Clerk of the Council. The Town Clerk, Metropolitan Borough of Poplar. The Public Health and Housing Committee were of opinion it is desirable the provisions of the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1908, enforcing cleanliness on premises where food is prepared for sale, or sold, should be applied also in the cases of barrows and stalls, and directed the London County Council be so informed. The Council approved the action of the Committee. 155 Conference to consider the Meat Supply of the Kingdom and the present restrictions on the importation of Live Cattle for Slaughter. The Greenwich Borough Council directed attention to the danger of the Foreign Beef supply of this country being ultimately controlled by a few American Companies, owing to the fact that live cattle can be imported into this country from North America only, and that these Companies control practically the whole of the live cattle trade, and had also acquired some of the larger undertakings for chilling and shipping beef from Argentina. It was suggested that the present restrictions on the importation of live cattle should be removed to admit of their importation, for slaughter at the port of landing, from countries officially declared free from disease. A Conference on the subject was convened, and the Public Health and Housing Committee appointed the then Chairman, Mr. A. H. Darby, with the Medical Officer of Health, to attend the Conference. The following resolution was adopted at the Conference of the Metropolitan Borough Councils, convened by the Greenwich Borough Council, at the Caxton Hall, Westminster, on the 12th October, on the subject of the meat supply of the Kingdom, viz.:— "This Conference, after carefully considering the information at its disposal, and bearing in mind the conclusions arrived at by the Departmental Committee appointed to inquire into combinations in the meat trade and also the serious rise which has taken place in the price of beef in the past two years, is of opinion that the meat supply of the Metropolis is in an extremely unsatisfactory position. "This Conference is further of opinion that owing to the failure of the home supply to increase in proportion to the national demand, the decrease in the numbers of live cattle imported and the increased necessity of depending upon the supplies of chilled and frozen meat, it is easier for the trade to be effectually controlled by large and powerful Trusts or Combinations of Companies. "This Conference is further of opinion that the time has arrived when the needs of large Urban Communities, which have 156 to rely to a great extent upon imported meat, call for a relaxation of the present restrictions upon the importation of foreign cattle for immediate slaughter in order that channels, other than tho3e now available, may be opened up for supplying this Country with meat. "That the Mayor of Westminster be requested to ask the Prime Minister to receive a Deputation on the subject in order that this Conference may place its views before the Government. "That copies of the above Resolution be sent to all Members of Parliament representing Metropolitan constituencies, to the Corporation of the City of London, the London County Council, and to the Corporations of Birmingham, Liverpool, Manchester and Glasgow, and other large Towns, and the Association of Municipal Corporations." The Public Health and Housing Committee recommended the Council that the resolution of the Conference be approved and supported. At a further Conference of Representatives of Metropolitan Borough Councils, convened by the Greenwich Borough Council to consider the Meat Supply of the Kingdom and the present Restrictions on the Importation of Live Cattle for Slaughter, held (by the courtesy of the Westminster City Council) at the Caxton Hall, Westminster, on Monday, the 28th February, 1910, the following resolutions were passed:— (a) "That the Mayor of Westminster be asked to approach the Prime Minister and urge him to reconsider his refusal to receive a Deputation as asked at the previous Conference"; (b) "That a copy of this Resolution be sent to the London Borough Councils asking whether they will support and are prepared to join in this Deputation": and 000000(c) "That copies of the above Resolution be sent to all Members of Parliament representing Metropolitan Constituencies, to the Corporation of the City of London, the London County Council, and to the Corporations, Cities and Boroughs which have replied to the circular letter sent by this Conference, and asked to use their influence with the Prime Minister to receive a Deputation." 157 HOUSES LET IN LODGINGS OR TO MEMBERS OF MORE THAN ONE FAMILY. Suggested further Legislation with respect to Furnished Rooms—Proposed License; London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1907, Section 78—Water Supply in Tenement Houses; London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1909—Storage of Food. At the end of the year there were 1,044 houses on the register. For Inspector's report see pages 245 to 248. Legal proceedings were instituted in three instances, see page 184. Suggested further Legislation with respect of Furnished Rooms—Proposed License. The following letter was submitted to the Public Health and Housing Committee:— London County Council, County Hall, Spring Gardens, S.W. 18th June, 1909. Sir, The Council has under consideration the question of the necessity for the exercise of increased control over a certain type of furnished room, the practice of letting such rooms for short periods, at a rate not exceeding 10s. a week, having recently become more general in various parts of the County of London. This class of room appears to be occupied by persons who are, if anything, of a more degraded character than those frequenting common lodging houses ; the rent is paid daily and the appointments of the rooms are mostly of a very scanty and dilapidated nature, the beds being, in many instances, verminous. The ease with which such rooms can be hired is, moreover, a great incentive to immorality. M 158 It appears to the Council that the bye-laws which have been made by the Councils of the several Metropolitan Boroughs under the provisions of Section 94 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, do not confer upon sanitary authorities adequate power for dealing with rooms of the above description. It is stated also that considerable difficulty is experienced in putting into operation the provisions of Part IV. of the London County Council (Genera' Powers) Act, 1904, as to the destruction of filthy or unwholesome articles, owing to the frequency with which disputes as to the amount of compensation for articles destroyed arise. The existence of these circumstances has led the Council to consider whether in the interest of public health, further legislation should be sought in respect of furnished rooms so let, to assure that they should be maintained with due regard to sanitation. It appears to the Council to be most desirable that greater supervision and control than exists at present should be exercised over this class of house, which is nearly related in character to the common lodging house, both in respect of the class of house and the social condition of the inmates, and, in fact, the demand for lodgings of this character exists, in the main, in localities where common lodging houses are situated, the lodging house and the furnished rooms not infrequently being owned by the same persons. The Council thinks that more effective control would be more ensured if persons letting such rooms were required to obtain an annual license, to be granted after consideration of the fitness of the applicant and the structure and cleanliness of the premises. Such licenses would be granted by the Council in the same manner as is done with regard to common lodging houses. An alternative means of securing adequate supervision would be by investing the local sanitary authority with full power to inspect and enforce a certain standard of requirements, authority being given to the Council, with the consent of the Local Government Board, in each case to act in default of action by the local sanitary authority. The Council would be glad if the Metropolitan Borough Councils would assist it in its deliberations by furnishing their views as to the means, by licensing or otherwise, by which the sanitary and moral condition of the inmates of these houses could be improved. The Council has no doubt but that this question has already been before the local sanitary authorities, and that 159 those authorities have in their possession valuable information bearing on the subject, and I have to state that the Council will be very glad to receive any information of this nature which is available. I am, Sir, Your obedient Servant, G. L. GOMME, The Town Clerk of the Clerk of the Council. Metropolitan Borough of Poplar. The Public Health and Housing Committee were informed there are very few furnished lodgings of the character indicated in the Borough. The Public Health and Housing Committee were of opinion increased powers of supervision are necessary in such cases, and that powers should be obtained by the London County Council for enforcement by Borough Councils of the washing of bed linen at least once a week, and as often as may be necessary; for requiring provision of sufficient washing accommodation, with towels, etc., and access to an ample water supply and to adequate sanitary conveniences. It is also desirable such premises should be licensed in order to ensure fitness of an applicant for a license, and of the premises for the purpose proposed, such license to be withdrawn where due care is not taken that the rooms are not used for immoral purposes. The Public Health and Housing Committee recommended the Council to concur in this opinion and the London County Council be so informed. London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1907, Section 78—Water Supply in Tenement Houses. In the month of March notices were served under this Act to provide a proper and sufficient water supply at some tenement houses in Bromley. After a short delay the work was carried out and there is now a supply tap and sink on each storey, which, needless to say, are greatly appreciated by the tenants. London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1909— Storage of Food. Part III. provides that in any tenement house not so used or occupied 160 before the passing of the Act, the Borough Council may require the owner to provide sufficient and suitable accommodation for storage of food for each family, on the floor or floors on which the rooms occupied by such family are situated, if such provision is practicable. Penalty for noncompliance 40s. and 20s. per day. ["Tenement house" means any house occupied by any person of the working class, wholly or partly let in lodgings or occupied by members of more than one family.] (See page 190.) HOUSING. Housing of the Working Classes Act, 1890, Section 30— Bird-in-Hand Court; Coventry Cross Place; "Black Beetle Alley"; Amendment of the definition of Owner; Conference on Town Planning; Housing, Town Planning, etc., Act, 1909. Housing of the Working Classes Act, 1890, Section 30— Bird-in-hand Court. Representations were made under Section 30 of the Housing of the Working Classes Act, 1890, that Nos. 1, 2, 3, 4, 5 and 6, Bird-in-hand Court were in such a state as to be dangerous or injurious to health, and unfit for human habitation. The Public Health and Housing Committee approved the service upon the owner of statutory notices that in default of the houses being made fit for human habitation within 14 days, legal proceedings would be taken to obtain closing orders. The Committee directed proceedings for closing orders be taken, in default, and application made to the Court for authority to pay the tenants of the houses reasonable allowances on account of their expenses of removal, such amount to be recovered from the owner by the Council. The Council approved the action of the Committee. Notices were served upon the owner, the houses were vacated by the tenants and the premises were made fit for human habitation. There was no need to institute legal proceedings. 161 Coventry Cross Place—"Black Beetle Alley." For several years past attention had been given to the sanitary condition of five small dwelling houses, four three-roomed and one tworoomed house, of the back-to-back and no through ventilation kind, respectively, which comprised a small block, Nos. 1, 2, 3, 4 and 5, Coventry Cross Place, colloquially known as "Black Beetle Alley." In December, 1906, proceedings were about to be taken for the closing of No. 4 (the tworoomed premises) which had fallen into a bad state of repair, but the owner closed this house and later on demolished it and cleared the site. Proceedings, no doubt, would have been instituted under the Housing of the Working Classes Act for closing the other four houses, had the owner not closed them. The houses in question were very old, decayed, worn out and were pulled down and site cleared. Amendment of the Definition of Owner. Difficulties having been experienced in taking proceedings under the Housing of the Working Classes Acts, by reason of inability to ascertain the name and address of the "owner" of the property affected (i.e., the person having not less than 21 years' unexpired interest in the premises), the Public Health and Housing Committee directed a communication be addressed to the President of the Local Government Board suggesting that the Housing and Town Planning Bill then before a Parliamentary Committee should be amended by the introduction of a clause on similar lines to those of Section 116 (3) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, to the effect that any person who collects the rents of, or is interested financially or otherwise in any premises, when requested by or on behalf of the Sanitary Authority to state the name and address of the owner of the premises or of any person interested financially or otherwise in such premises, who refuses or wilfully omits to disclose, or wilfully mis-states the same shall be liable to a penalty. The Council approved the action of the Committee. See Housing, Town Planning, &c., Act, 1909, Section 49 (2), amendment of definitions for purpose of Part II. of the principal Act. Conference on Town Planning. The Garden Cities and Town Planning Association convened a Conference on Town Planning and its practical application, held at the 162 Guildhall on December 10th; the Lord Mayor presided and invited appointment of delegates. The Public Health and Housing Committee appointed the Chairman and the Medical Officer of Health to attend the Conference. At the Conference on Town Planning convened by the Garden Cities and Town Planning Association, it was agreed that a Central Consultative Town Planning Body should be formed for the purpose of giving advice and assistance upon all matters connected with Town Planning and the better development of our towns. The Council was invited to send representatives to the first meeting of the proposed Consultative Body. The Public Health and Housing Committee recommended that Mr. Councillor A. H. Warren be appointed the Council's representative at the first meeting of the proposed Consultative Body on Town Planning. Housing, Town Planning, &c., Act, 1909. (9 Edw. 7. Chap. 44.) An Act to amend the Law relating to the Housing of the Working Classes, to provide for the making of Town Planning schemes, and to make further provision with respect to the appointment and duties of County Medical Officers of Health, and to provide for the establishment of Public Health and Housing Committees of County Councils. This Act was passed on the 3rd December, 1909, and its short title and extent enacted by Section 76 (1) is as follows:- This Act may be cited as the Housing Town Planning, &c., Act, 1909, and Part I. of this Act shall be construed as one with the Housing of the Working Classes Acts, 1890 to 1903, and that Part of this Act and those Acts may be cited together as the Housing of the Working Classes Acts, 1890 to 1909. The Sections which affect the Metropolitan Borough Councils are as follow:— 163 Part I. Housing of the Working Classes. Facilities for Acquisition of Lands and other Purposes of the Housing Acts. Part III. of the principal Act to take effect without adoption. 53 & 54 Vict. c. 70. 1. Part III. of the Housing of the Working Classes Act, 1890 (in this Part of this Act referred to as the principal Act), shall, after the commencement of this Act, extend to and take effect in every urban or rural district, or other place for which it has not been adopted, as if it had been so adopted. Powers of enforcing Execution of Housing Acts. Power of Local Government Board. 10.—(1) Where a complaint is made to the Local Government Board— (c) as respects the area of any other local authority by four inhabitant householders of the area; that the local authority have failed to exereise their powers under Part II. or Part III. of the principal Act in cases where those powers ought to have been exercised, the Board may cause a public local inquiry to be held, and if, after holding such an inquiry, the Board are satisfied that there has been such a failure on the part of the local authority, the Board may declare the authority to be in default, and may make an order directing that authority, within a time limited by the order, to carry out such works and do such other things as may be mentioned in the order for the purpose of remedying the default. Contracts by Landlord. Extension of Section 75 of the principal Act. 14. In any contract made after the passing of this Act for letting for habitation a house or part of a house at a rent not exceeding— 164 (a) in the case of a house situate in the administrative county of London, forty pounds ; (b) in the case of a house situate in a borough or urban district with a population according to the last census for the time being of fifty thousand or upwards, twenty-six pounds; (c) in the case of a house situate elsewhere, sixteen pounds; there shall be implied a condition that the house is at the commencement of the holding in all respects reasonably fit for human habitation, but the condition aforesaid shall not be implied when a house or part of a house is let for a term of not less than three years upon the terms that it be put by the lessee into a condition reasonably fit for occupation, and the lease is not determinable at the option of either party before the expiration of that term. Condition as to keeping houses let to persons of the working classes in repair. 15.—(1) The last foregoing section shall, as respects contracts to which that section applies, take effect as if the condition implied by that section included an undertaking that the house shall, during the holding, be kept by the landlord in all respects reasonably fit for human habitation. (2) The landlord or the local authority, or any person authorised by him or them in writing, may at reasonable times of the day, on giving twenty-four hours' notice in writing to the tenant or occupier, enter any house, premises, or building to which this section applies for the purpose of viewing the state and condition thereof. (3) If it appears to the local authority within the meaning of Part II. of the principal Act that the undertaking implied by virtue of this section is not complied with in the case of any house to which it applies, the authority shall, if a closing order is not made with respect to the house, by written notice require the landlord, within a reasonable time, not being less than twentyone days, specified in the notice, to execute such works as the authority shall specify in the notice as being necessary to make the house in all respects reasonably fit for human habitation. 165 (4) Within twenty-one days after the receipt of such notice the landlord may by written notice to the local authority declare his intention of closing the house for human habitation, and thereupon a closing order shall be deemed to have become operative in respect of such house. (5) If the notice given by the local authority is not complied with, and if the landlord has not given the notice mentioned in the immediately preceding subsection, the authority may, at the expiration of the time specified in the notice given by them to the landlord, do the work required to be done and recover the expenses incurred by them in so doing from the landlord as a civil debt in manner provided by the Summary Jurisdiction Acts, or, if they think fit, the authority may by order declare any such expenses to be payable by annual instalments within a period not exceeding that of the interest of the landlord in the house, nor in any case five years, with interest at a rate not exceeding five pounds per cent. per annum, until the whole amount is paid, and any such instalments or interest or any part thereof may be recovered from the landlord as a civil debt in manner provided by the Summary Jurisdiction Acts. (6) A landlord may appeal to the Local Government Board against any notice requiring him to execute works under this section, and against any demand for the recovery of expenses from him under this section or order made with respect to those expenses under this section by the authority, by giving notice of appeal to the Board within twenty-one days after the notice is received, or the demand or order is made, as the case may be, and no proceedings shall be taken in respect of such notice requiring works, order, or demand, whilst the appeal is pending. (7) In this section the expression "landlord" means any person who lets to a tenant for habitation the house under any contract referred to in this section, and includes his successors in title; and the expression "house" includes part of a house. (8) Sections forty-nine and fifty of the principal Act as amended by section thirteen of the Housing of the Working Classes Act, 1903 (which relate to the service of notices and the description of owner in proceedings), shall apply for the purposes of this section, with the substitution, where required, of the landlord for the owner of a dwelling-house. 166 (9) Any remedy given by this section for non-compliance with the undertaking implied by virtue of this section shall be in addition to and not in derogation of any other remedy available to the tenant against the landlord, either at common law or otherwise. Amendment of Procedure for Closing Orders and Demolition Orders. Duty of local authority as to closing of dwelling-house unfit for human habitation. 17.—(1) It shall be the duty of every local authority within the meaning of Part II. of the principal Act to cause to be made from time to time inspection of their district, with a view to ascertain whether any dwelling-house therein is in a state so dangerous or injurious to health as to be unfit for human habitation, and for that purpose it shall be the duty of the local authority, and of every officer of the local authority, to comply with such regulations and to keep such records as may be prescribed by the Board. (2) If, on the representation of the medical officer of health, or of any other officer of the authority, or other information given, any dwelling-house appears to them to be in such a state, it shall be their duty to make an order prohibiting the use of the dwelling-house for human habitation (in this Act referred to as a closing order) until in the judgment of the local authority the dwelling-house is rendered fit for that purpose. (3) Notice of a closing-order shall be forthwith served on every owner of the dwelling-house in respect of which it is made, and any owner aggrieved by the order may appeal to the Local Government Board by giving notice of appeal to the Board within fourteen days after the order is served upon him. (4) Where a closing order has become operative, the local authority shall serve notice of the order on every occupying tenant of the dwelling-house in respect of which the order is made, and, within such period as is specified in the notice, not being less than fourteen days after the service of the notice, the order shall be obeyed by him, and he and his family shall cease to inhabit the dwelling-house, and in default he shall be liable 167 on summary conviction to be ordered to quit the dwelling-house within such time as may be specified in the order. (5) Unless the dwelling-house has been made unfit for habitation by the wilful act or default of the tenant or of any person for whom as between himself and the owner or landlord he is responsible, the local authority may make to every such tenant such reasonable allowance on account of his expense in removing as may be determined by the local authority with the consent of the owner of the dwelling-house, or, if the owner of the dwellinghouse fails to consent to the sum determined by the local authority, as may be fixed by a court of summary jurisdiction, and the amount of the said allowance shall be recoverable by the local authority from the owner of the dwelling-house as a civil debt in manner provided by the Summary Jurisdiction Acts. (6) The local authority shall determine any closing order made by them if they are satisfied that the dwelling-house, in respect of which the order has been made, has been rendered fit for human habitation. If, on the application of any owner of a dwelling-house, the local authority refuse to determine a closing order, the owner may appeal to the Local Government Board by giving notice of appeal to the Board within fourteen days after the application is refused. Underground Room. (7) A room habitually used as a sleeping place, the surface of the floor of which is more than three feet below the surface of the part of the street adjoining or nearest to the room, shall for the purposes of this section be deemed to be a dwelling-house so dangerous or injurious to health as to be unfit for human habitation, if the room either— (a) is not on an average at least seven feet in height from floor to ceiling; or (b) does not comply with such regulations as the local authority with the consent of the Local Government Board may prescribe for securing the proper ventilation and lighting of such rooms, and the protection thereof against dampness, effluvia, or exhalation: provided that if the local authority, after being required to do so by the Local 168 Government Board, fail to make such regulations, or such regulations as the Board approve, the Board may themselves make them, and the regulations so made shall have effect as if they had been made by the local authority with the consent of the Board : Provided that a closing order made in respect of a room to which this subsection applies shall not prevent the room being used for purposes other than those of a sleeping place; and that, if the occupier of the room after notice of an order has been served upon him fails to comply with the order, an order to comply therewith may, on summary conviction, be made against him. This subsection shall not come into operation until the first day of July nineteen hundred and ten, and a closing order mads in respect of any room to which this subsection applies shall not be treated as a closing order in respect of a dwelling-house for the purposes of the next succeeding section. Order for demolition. 18.—(1) Where a closing order in respect of any dwellinghouse has remained operative for a period of three months, the local authority shall take into consideration the question of the demolition of the dwelling-house, and shall give every owner of the dwelling-house notice of the time (being some time not less than one month after the service of the notice) and place at which the question will be considered, and any owner of the dwelling-house shall be entitled to be heard when the question is so taken into consideration. (2) If upon any such consideration the local authority are of opinion that the dwelling-house has not been rendered fit for human habitation, and that the necessary steps are not being taken with all due diligence to render it so fit, or that the continuance of any building, being or being part of the dwellinghouse, is a nuisance or dangerous or injurious to the health of the public or of the inhabitants of the neighbouring dwellinghouses, they shall order the demolition of the building. (3) If any owner undertakes to execute forthwith the works necessary to render the dwelling-house fit for human habitation, and the local authority consider that it can be so rendered fit for human habitation, the local authority may, if they think fit, 169 postpone the operation of the order for such time, not exceeding six months, as they think sufficient for the purpose of giving the owner an opportunity of executing the necessary works. (4) Notice of an order for the demolition of a building shall be forthwith served on every owner of the building in respect of which it is made, and any owner aggrieved by the order may appeal to the Local Government Board by giving notice of appeal to the Board within twenty-one days after the order is served upon him. Power to redeem annuities charged by charging order under section 36 of the principal Act. 19. Any owner of or other person interested in a dwellinghouse on which an annuity has been charged by a charging order made under section thirty-six of the principal Act (which relates to the grant of charges) shall at any time be at liberty to redeem the annuity on payment to the person entitled to the annuity of such sum as may be agreed upon, or in default of agreement determined by the Local Government Board. Provision as to priority of charges under section 37 of the principal Act. 20. The charges excepted in subsection (1) of section thirtyseven of the principal Act (which relates to the incidence of charges) shall include charges on the dwelling-house created or arising under any provision of the Public Health Acts, or under any provision in any local Act authorising a charge for recovery of expenses incurred by a local authority. Restriction on poiver of court of summary jurisdiction to extend time. 21. Subsection (3) of section forty-seven of the principal Act (which gives power to a court of summary jurisdiction to enlarge the time for certain matters) shall cease to have effect as respects the time allowed for the execution of any works or the demolition of a building under a closing order or under an order for the demolition of a building. 170 General Amendments. Power of Entry. 36.—Any person authorised in writing stating the particular purpose or purposes for which the entry is authorised, by the local authority or the Local Government Board, may at all reasonable times, on giving twenty-four hours' notice to the occupier and to the owner, if the owner is known, of his intention, enter any house, premises, or buildings— (a) for the purpose of survey or valuation, in the case of houses, premises, or buildings, which the local authority are authorised to purchase compulsorily under the Housing Acts; and (b) for the purpose of survey and examination in the case of any dwelling-house in respect of which a closing order or an order for demolition has been made; or (c) for the purpose of survey and examination, where it appears to the authority or Board that survey or examination is necessary in order to determine whether any powers under the Housing Acts should be exercised in respect of any house, premises, or building. Notice may be given to the occupier for the purpose of this section by leaving a notice addressed to the occupier, without name or further description, at the house, buildings, or premises. Power of Local Government Board to obtain a report on any crowded area. 37. If it appears to the Local Government Board that owing to density of population, or any other reason, it is expedient to inquire into the circumstances of any area with a view to determining whether any powers under the Housing Acts should be put into force in that area or not, the Local Government Board may require the local authority to make a report to them containing such particulars as to the population of the district and other matters as they direct, and the local authority shall comply with the requirement of the Local Government Board, and any expenses incurred by them in so doing shall be paid as expenses incurred in the execution of such Part of the principal Act as the Local Government Board determine. 171 Prohibition of back-to-back houses. 43. Notwithstanding anything in any local Act or bye-law in force in any borough or district, it shall not be lawful to erect any back to-back houses intended to be used as dwellings for the working classes, and any such house commenced to be erected after the passing of this Act shall be deemed to be unfit for human habitation for the purposes of the provisions of the Housing Acts. Provided that nothing in this section— (a) shall prevent the erection or use of a house containing several tenements in which the tenements are placed back to back, if the medical officer of health for the district certifies that the several tenements are so constructed and arranged as to secure effective ventilation of all habitable rooms in every tenement; or (b) shall apply to houses abutting on any streets the plans whereof have been approved by the local authority before the first day of May nineteen hundred and nine, in any borough or district in which, at the passing of this Act, any local Act or bye-laws are in force permitting the erection of back-to-back houses. Definitions. Provisions of this Part to be deemed to be part of the appropriate Part of the principal Act. 47.—(1) Any provisions of this Act which supersede or amend any provisions of the principal Act shall be deemed to be part of that Part of the principal Act in which the provisions superseded or amended are contained. (2) Any reference in the Housing Acts to a closing order or to an order for] the demolition of a building shall be construed as a reference to a closing order or an order of demolition under this Act. Amendment of definitions in Part I of the principal Act. 48. The expression "street" shall, unless the context 172 wise requires, have the same meaning in Part I. of the principal Act as it has in Part II. of that Act, and shall include any court, alley, street, square, or row of houses. Amendment of definitions for purpose of Part II. of the principal Act. 49.—(1) The words "means any inhabited building and" shall be omitted from the definition of "dwelling-house" in section twenty-nine of the principal Act. (2) For the definition of owner in the same section the following definition shall be substituted:— "The expression 'owner,' in addition to the definition given by the Lands Clauses Acts, includes all lessees or mortgagees of any premises required to be dealt with under this Part of this Act, except persons holding or entitled to the rents and profits of such premises under a lease the original term whereof is less than twenty-one years." Definition of cottage. 50. For the definition of cottage in section 53 of the principal Act the following definition shall be substituted:— "The expression 'cottage' in this Part of this Act may include a garden of not more than one acre." Definition of Housing Acts. 51. In this Part of this Act the expression "Housing Acts" means the principal Act, and any Act amending that Act, including this Act. Part II. Town Planning. Preparation and approval of town planning scheme. 54.—(1) A town planning scheme may be made in accordance with the provisions of this Part of this Act as respects any land 173 which is in course of development or appears likely to be used for building purposes, with the general object of securing proper sanitary conditions, amenity, and convenience in connexion with the laying out and use of the land, and of any neighbouring lands. Application to London. 66.—(1) This Part of this Act shall apply to the administrative county of London, and, as respects that county, the London County Council shall be the local authority. Part III. County Medical Officers, County Public Health and Housing Committee, &c. Extent of Part III. 70. The foregoing provisions of this Part of this Act shall not apply to Scotland or, except subsection (4) of section sixtyeight, to the administrative county of London, and, in the application of the said subsection to London, the reference to a medical officer of health of a district shall be construed as a reference to the medical officer of health of a metropolitan borough. (4) The medical officer of health of a county shall, for the purposes of his duties, have the same powers of entry on premises as are conferred on a medical officer of health of a district by or under any enactment. Formation and extension of building societies. 72.—(1) The county council may promote the formation or extension of and may, subject to the provisions of this section, assist societies on a co-operative basis, having for their object or one of their objects the erection or improvement of dwellings for the working classes. (2) The county council, with the consent of and subject to the regulations made by the Local Government Board, may for the purpose of assisting a society make grants or advances to the society, or guarantee advances made to the society, upon such n 174 terms and conditons as to rate of interest and repayment, or otherwise, and on such security, as the council think fit, and the making of such grants or advances shall be a purpose for which a council may borrow: Provided that the regulations of the Board shall provide that any such advance made on the security of any property shall not exceed two-thirds of the value of that property. Part IV. Supplemental. Provisions as to commons and open spaces. WATER CERTIFICATES. Water certificates were authorised to be given under Section 48 (2) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891—20, King Street, Poplar; 18, 22 and 24, Jebb Street, Bow; 42, 44, 46, 48 and 50, Blondin Street, Bow; 3, 5, 7, 9 and 11, Priory Street; 6, Bidgdale Street; 3 new houses and a shop in Wade Street; 728, Old Ford Road; 48, 52, 54, 56, 58 and 60, Wrexham Road; School-keeper's House, British Street School, Poplar; New L.C.C. Schools, Dingle Lane, Poplar; New School-keeper's House, Marner Street Schools. SEWERAGE AND DRAINAGE. No. 1, Duff Street and the Mercantile Marine Offices. In November, 1908, owing to trouble with the drains of No. 1, Duff Street, which was traced on to the Mercantile Marine Offices, East India Dock Road, H.M. Office of Works claimed the brick barrel, which took the drainage of both premises, to be a sewer, and suggested the barrel should be re-constructed throughout its entire length (140 feet) with pipes. Upon examination the brickwork was found to be in fairly good condition, though the flow, which was necessarily small, was sluggish. 175 No work was carried out then except to place two manholes in the street for access and cleansing, but H.M. Office of Works pressed for re construction and proposed to entirely redrain their own premises. Negotiations were carried out on a suggestion of the Borough Surveyor that in consideration of the diversion of the drain of No. 1, Duff Street, and the demolition of the barrel by the Council, H.M. Office of Works should relieve the Council of all liability in respect thereof, and construct the new drainage of their premises at their own expense, and this was agreed to on behalf of H.M. Office of Works. The Borough Surveyor proposed to divert the drainage of No. 1, Duff Street into the combined drainage system at No. 3, Duff Street, already maintainable by the Council, and the owners concerned agreed to this course. The Public Health and Housing Committee were of opinion the proposal of the Borough Surveyor should be adopted, and recommended the Council:— "That the drainage of No. 1, Duff Street be disconnected from the brick barrel at the Mercantile Marine Offices, East India Dock Eoad, and diverted into the combined drainage system of No. 3, Duff Street; and the brick barrel referred to be demolished." The Bill was introduced into the House of Commons on the 5th March, 1908, by Mr. Dickinson and was read a first time. The Bill has not been brought before the present House of Commons owing to the position of affairs at Westminster. It is a Private Member's Bill and appears to be very questionable whether it will become law without the co-operation of the Government for the time being. During the last Parliament, from March, 1908, to July, 1909, the Bill was tabled over 100 occasions without being read a second time. The Bill is set out on page 193 in extenso. CUSTOMS AND INLAND REVENUE ACTS, 1890-1. Cap. 8, Sect. 26 (2.) REVENUE ACT, 1903. 3 Edw. 7, Cap. 46. Rothbtjry Mansions, Bow—Regranting Certificate. In 1908 Rothbury Buildings,* Bow, were found to be in a most insanitary condition ; there was also no proper and sufficient supply of waterf with no adequate washing accommodation. The Public Health and Housing Committee directed that the Board of Inland Revenue be informed of the facts and that exemption under the Acts should no longer exist. The Board of Inland Revenue thereupon treated the certificate as cancelled. At the time of writing this report the owner is carrying out extensive work in order that certificates shall be granted. * Now called Rothbury Mansions. † London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1907. 177 LONDON COUNTY COUNCIL (GENERAL POWERS) ACTS, 1904 & 1909. Verminous Rooms* and Licensed Premises' Urinals; Power to provide Sanitary Conveniences jointly with adjoining Boroughs. Verminous Rooms. Verminous rooms are still brought to the notice of owners upon the "Intimations" which are followed, if necessary, by "Statutory Notices," more especially as there are generally other defects upon the premises, and certainly verminous premises are "in such a state as to be a nuisance to health," and owners, if they do not already know, are informed when defects are being dealt with under the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, that the vermin must be got rid of, otherwise the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1904, will be brought into operation. When a notice is served for cleansing under the bye-laws for houses let in lodgings or occupied by members of more than one family, should the premises be verminous a special intimation to that effect is enclosed. The above procedure saves a large amount of time, as under the London County Council (General Powers) Act a certificate must be given by the Medical Officer of Health, who would have to inspect every room. However, eleven certificates had to be given to the Public Health and Housing Committee. During the year 232 rooms have been cleansed of vermin:— Poplar South Division 11 West Combined Division 15 East Combined Division 14 Bromley Central Division 11 Bromley North Division 20 Bow West Division 21 Bow East Division 36 Houses let in lodgings 104 No summonses were applied for. * For the Cleansing of Verminous Persons see page 89. 178 Licensed Premises' Urinals. Four notices were served under Section 22 of the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1904, calling upon the owners to provide proper doors to the urinals attached to their licensed premises. London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1909. By Section 66 power is given to Metropolitan Borough Councils to provide sanitary Conveniences on or near their boundaries jointly with the Councils of adjoining Boroughs or Districts. SCHOOLS. Inspection of Schools; the use of Playgrounds of Public Elementary Schools for the purposes of Recreation. Inspection of Schools. The Schools of the Borough were inspected and the sanitary conveniences and water supplies were found in a satisfactory condition. The use of Playgrounds of Public Elementary Schools for the purposes of recreation. The following letter was submitted to the Public Health and Housing Committee:— St. Pancras Borough Council, The Town Hall, Pancras Road, N.W. 19th January, 1909. Dear Sir, Children's Playgrounds. The attention of the Council has been directed to the danger incurred by children being compelled by circumstances to use the public streets, in which frequently there is heavy vehicular traffic, as playgrounds. In numerous cases the children reside in localities 179 far removed from a public park, garden or open space, and it is not practicable, therefore, for them to often visit these recreation grounds. The Council have considered a suggestion that it is very desirable that further grounds should be provided in London for children so that they may be able to play in safety therein without being exposed to the dangers of the traffic in the public thoroughfares, and a Committee of the Council has been appointed to consider the question in regard to the use of a portion of the public gardens in this Borough for the purpose. Attention has also been called to the playgrounds of the Schools under the control of the London County Council, which are only open and available at the present time to the scholars attending those Schools immediately before and after school hours, and during the short recreation periods during the school hours. In the opinion of the Council it is very desirable in the children's interest that the County Council should be urged to throw open these playgrounds out of school hours to all children for recreation purposes. I am instructed, therefore, to ask if your Council would be prepared to co-operate by joining in a petition to the County Council praying that the playgrounds attached to the Public Elementary Schools under their jurisdiction should be thrown open out of school hours for the use of all children who may wish to use them for the purposes of recreation. Will you kindly let me know at an early date the views of your Authority in regard to the matter. Yours faithfully, C. H. F. BARRETT, Town Clerk. L. Potts, Esq., Town Clerk, Poplar. The Public Health and Housing Committee recommended the Council to join in the petition suggested in the foregoing letter. 180 SANITARY INSPECTORS' WORK, OVERCROWDING, &c., AND POLICE COURT PROCEEDINGS. Cul-de-sac off Mellish Street, Dock Cottages, Prince Arthur's Avenue; Closure of Duff's Fields; Removal of Obnoxious and Offensive Matter—Proceedings by the Works Department. In two cases where w.c. pans and traps were fixed without notice being given under Clause 14 of the London County Council Bye-laws, Section 39(1) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, the builders were written to asking them to open the ground, otherwise legal proceedings would be instituted against them for infringing the bye-law. This mode of procedure prevents good work being constructed on to defective work. Whenever drainage work has been found to have been done and covered up without inspection, if the builder will not open the ground, the work is exposed by the Sanitary Authority, and such a line of action ensures sound work. One other case came under the notice of the Public Health Department. This case was reported to the Borough Surveyor as no plans had been submitted, a new w.c. with drains having been constructed. 181 List of Sanitary Works carried out in the Metropolitan Borough of Poplar, from January 1st, 1909, to December .31st, 1909.* Poplar South. Dock Cottages. Prince Arthur's Avenue. In December, 1908, the Magistrate dismissed a summons taken against the owner of a cul-de-sac off Mellish Street, to enforce proper drainage and repair of such place, which was in bad condition and drained into untrapped gullies. The proceedings were taken under Section 100 of the Metropolis Management Act, 1855, and the effect of the decision is that unless the freeholder is also the owner of the houses abutting no proceedings can be effective to compel owners to repair such places. The Committee were advised an appeal was not likely to be successful, and therefore directed no further legal action be taken in the matter. The Public Health and Housing Committee also requested the Works Committee to consider the desirability of arranging for the necessary work to be done by the Council at the place in question, and at other places in similar cases, the cost being apportioned upon the owners. Early in the year 1909, the Works Committee reported to the Council that the Midland Railway Company bad agreed to repair the thoroughfare at Dock Cottages. Negotiations were proceeding with the owners in respect of the making up of Prince Arthur's Avenue. With regard to the cul de sac between Nos. 67 and 69, Mellish Street, Millwall, liability was accepted by two out of the three owners of the property abutting thereon, but was disputed by the third. The Works Committee were of opinion 183 it was desirable the road should be repaired, but that no liability for future maintenance should be undertaken by the Council, and recommended:— "That the necessary repairs to the cul de sac between 67 and 69, Mellish Street be carried out by the Borough Surveyor, and the cost apportioned upon, and recovered from, the owners, in accordance with the powers of the Council under Section 3 of the Metropolis Management Amendment Act, 1890." Closure of Duff's Fields. The Works Committee reported to the Council that they had been waited upon by a deputation, who pointed out to them certain improper uses to which a passageway known as Duff's Fields was put. The Works Committee were assured the owners would do all that is necessary for the convenience of the tenants, who would remain in occupation, by putting up a gate and providing them with keys, so that they would be able to use the back portions of the premises Nos. 25 and 27, St. Leonards Road, as previously. The Works Committee were of opinion the thoroughfare in question might well be closed without any injury or inconvenience to the general public, and the Council agreed to the proposal that the passageway known as Duffs Fields, situate between St. Leonards Road and Lodore Street, should be closed as desired by the owners. After the usual formalities Duff's Fields was closed in the manner suggested above. 184 Police Court Proceedings. No. o£ Street or Name of Premises or Locality, Ac. Nature of Complaint. Metropolitan Ambulances Act, 1909, 9 Edw. VII., cap. 17; Trade Boards Act, 1909, 9 Edw. VIP., cap. 22; Oaths Act, 1909, 9 Edw. VII., cap. 39; Housing, Town Planning, etc., Act, 1909, 9 Edw. VII., cap. 44; London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1909, 9 Edw. VII., ch. cxxx. Metropolitan Ambulances Act, 1909, 9 Edw. VII., cap. 17. "In 1906 the London County Council sought powers to establish and maintain, and to aid the establishment and maintenance of, an efficient ambulance service for cases of street accident and illness occurring in London. The Clauses of the Bill, after examination by the Police and Sanitary Committee, passed the House of Commons but failed to pass the House of Lords. "The Home Secretary thereupon appointed a Departmental Committee ' to inquire as to the provision made for dealing with cases of accident and sudden illness occurring in streets and public places in the Metropolis, and to report whether any, and, if so, what, improvements in ambulance provision are necessary or desirable, and how they could be best effected with due regard to efficiency ami economy.' "The Committee reported that it had been' abundantly shown that the present system is gravely defective and results in much preventible 186 detriment and suffering by reason of the transport, by unsuitable means, of persons who have been injured or taken ill in the streets or other public places; and that the real evil arises from the great use which is made of ordinary vehicles, such as cabs and vans, even in serious cases, which require the transport of the patient by the best means possible.' "The Committee was divided in opinion as to whether the London County Council or Metropolitan Asylums Board should be the authority for ambulance purposes in London. "The present statute, which was introduced into the House of Commons by Sir William Collins, reproduces the powers sought by the London County Council three years ago, in order to enable them to provide for London such ambulance service as the Departmental Committee's investigations have shown to be required." (Paterson's Practical Statutes.) Trade Boards Act, 1909. 9 Edw. VII., Cap. 22. "This statute enforces, subject to elaborate restrictions, the novel principle of a minimum rate of wages in certain industries, commonly known as ' sweated ' industries. These industries are (1) those specified in the schedule (namely, ready-made and wholesale tailoring, the making of paper or chip boxes, machine lace making and chain making); and also (2) any other trade with exceptionally low wages to which the Act may be applied by the Board of Trade under a provisional order requiring the consent of Parliament. "The method for fixing a minimum wage is by means of Trade Boards, constituted under regulations by the Board of Trade, consisting of an equal number of representatives, members of employers and workers> together with appointed members whose number must be less than half the total of representative members. It is the duty of Trade Boards to fix minimum time-rates of wages, and they are also empowered to fix general minimum piece-rates of wages. These minimum rates do not become obligatory until sanctioned by an order of the Board of Trade made after an interval of six months ; but they have at once a limited operation (1) in the absence of a definite agreement; (2) when an employer has given notice that he is willing to adopt them ; and (3) in the case of contracts with a government department or a local authority. "Power is given to Trade Boards to establish district trade committees, with a constitution similar to their own, to which they may delegate any of their powers and duties except that of fixing minimum 187 rates of wages. Women are made eligible as members of Trade Boards both representative and appointed, and in the case of a trade in which women are largely employed at least one of the appointed members acting must be a woman. The Board of Trade is empowered to appoint Officers for carrying out the Act, who may be the existing inspectors of factories under the Home Department; and it is expressly provided that, by Order in Council, an interchange of powers may be effected between the several departments of Government concerned. Finally the expenses of Trade Boards and of their representative members (including compensation for loss of time) and also the remuneration of appointed members and secretaries, are to be paid out of monies provided by Parliament." (Paterson's Practical Statutes.) Oaths Act, 1909, 9 Edw. VII., cap. 39. "By Section 5 of the Oaths Act, 1888, it is open to a person to whom an oath is administered to swear, if he so desires, with unlifted hand, in the form and manner in which an oath is usually administered in Scotland. Under that Act, however, judges, magistrates, coroners and other persons do not consider themselves justified in suggesting this form of oath to the witness, jury or deponent; and it is a question whether the oath ought to be so administered unless the witness, etc., voluntarily request it to be done. Consequently, the old form of kissing the Book, to which there are many objections, still prevails. "The object of the present statute, which was introduced into the House of Commons by Mr. Bramsdon, but was considerably amended in Committee and also in the House of Lords, is to endeavour to make universal the administration of the oath according to a new method— namely, with the Book in the uplifted hand—by enabling the court, etc., to administer it in this form unless the person about to take the oath voluntarily objects thereto." (Paterson's Practical Statutes.) Housing, Town Planning, etc., Act, 1909, 9 Edw. VII., cap 44. "The main objects of this Statute, which was for two years under discussion by the House of Commons, and was also subjected to modification in the House of Lords are twofold: (1) to amend the existing law relating to the housing of the working classes by conferring wider powers, simplifying the procedure and strengthening the authority of the Local Government Board; (2) to introduce an entirely new method of urban 188 and suburban development, by means of carefully thought out schemes called ' town planning.' "Part I. of the statute, dealing with the housing of the working classes, while in form it consists only of amendments to the Act of 1890, substantially transforms that Act into a measure of much greater efficiency. Instead of being an adoptive Act it is now made operative in every urban and rural district. Whenever a complaint is made by four householders that the local authority have failed to exercise their powers, the Local Government Board may order them to do their duty within a limited time, and such order may be enforced by mandamus. Similar powers are also given to a county council to take action in case of default. Land may be acquired compulsorily in accordance with the simpler procedure first authorised under the Small Holdings and Allotments Acts, 1907. The period for the repayment of a loan from the Public Works Loans Commissioners is extended from 50 to 80 years, and the money is to be advanced at the lowest rate of interest allowed at the time. "With regard to the implied contract by a landlord that a house is reasonably fit for human habitation, this implied contract is now made applicable to all houses (with no restrictions to those for the working classes only) let at the following rents:—In London, £40 ; in a borough or urban district council with a population of 50,000, £26; elsewhere, £16. Moreover, this implied contract, which before only applied to the condition^ of the house at the time of letting, is now extended to the duration of the holding, and the local authority is empowered to see that the contract is duly carried out. "With regard to the closing or demolition of dwelling houses unfit for human habitation, in place of the former procedure of recourse to a court of summary jurisdiction with appeal to quarter sessions, an administrative order by the local authority is substituted, with appeal to the Local Government Board. Special provision is made for rooms below the surface of the street, habitually used as sleeping places; the erection of back-to-back houses as dwellings for the working classes is prohibited; and the definition of a cottage is extended so as to include a garden of not more than one acre, without regard to the annual value of such garden. "With regard to improvement and reconstruction schemes, an order of the Local Government Board sanctioning such scheme, and authorising compulsory purchase of land, is no longer a provisional order requiring confirmation by Parliament, but takes effect when sanctioned. "Powers are given to the Local Government Board to enter any 189 house for the purpose of survey or valuation, and also to obtain a report upon any crowded area from the local authority. For hearing appeals the Board is required to make rules, which must provide that no appeal be dismissed without a public local inquiry; and at any stage in the proceedings a special case may be stated for the opinion of the High Court on a point of law. "Part II. deals with the novel question of "town planning": that is, the provision of a considered scheme for the laying out of land which is in course of development or appears likely to be used for building purposes. It is, of course, open to private persons, including landowners, to prepare such a scheme ; but the statute contains elaborate provisions for the preparation or adoption of town planning schemes by a local authority—either a borough or a district council and the London County Council. Such schemes must contain provisions prescribed by the Local Government Board; they must be approved by the Board, and in the event of opposition they must be likewise laid before Parliament. The scheme may include power to purchase land compulsorily in accordance with the simplified procedure provided in Part I. In case of default by the local authority the Local Government Board may issue orders enforceable by madamus. "Parts III. and IV. contain general provisions. Of these the most important is that which makes compulsory (instead of optional, as hitherto) the duty of every county council to appoint a medical officer of health, who may not engage in private practice or hold any other public appointment, whose duties are to include those prescribed generally by the Local Government Board, and who may not be dismissed without the consent of the Board. Every county council (except that of London) is required to establish a public health and housing committee, to whom all relevant matters must be referred and whose reports must be considered. Provision is aho made for assistance to be given to co-operative building societies. Finally, no common land or open space may be acquired under this statute or under the Housing Acts unless the scheme authorising it is expressly confirmed by Parliament." (Paterson's Practical Statutes.) (See page 162.) O 190 London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1909, 9 Edw. VII., ch. cxxx. Part III.—Accommodation for Storage of Food. 16. If at any time it appears to any sanitary authority that in any tenement house within their district sufficient and suitable accommodation for the storage of food is not provided for the use of each family occupying such house on the storey or one of the storeys in which are situate the rooms or lodgings in the separate occupation of such family, the sanitary authority may, if the provision of such accommodation is practisable, cause notice to be served on the owner of such house requiring him, within such reasonable time as may be specified in the notice, to provide sufficient and suitable accommodation for the purpose aforesaid, and any owner failing to comply with such requirement within the period prescribed in the notice shall be liable, on summary conviction, to a penalty not exceeding forty shillings and to a daily penalty not exceeding twenty shillings. Provided that this Section shall not apply to any tenement house used or occupied as such before the passing of this Act. 17.
